Nia’s expression softened with recognition. “Yeah. That means your brain doesn’t trust the concept yet. So we’re not going to start with steps.”

She turned to a blank page and drew a simple shape. Then another. Then arrows between them. Her hands moved quickly, but her voice stayed calm.

“Look,” she said. “If the professor writes it one way, that’s not the whole idea. That’s one doorway into it. The real question is what the math is trying to describe. Once you see that, the notation stops feeling like a trap.”

Keisha stared at the page. “You make it sound less scary.”

Nia laughed. “It’s still scary. That’s part of the charm.”

Keisha laughed too, and the tension broke.

This was becoming one of Nia’s quiet gifts at FAMU—not just understanding difficult material, but helping other people feel like they were allowed to understand it too. In another environment, such moments might have remained isolated acts of kindness. Here, inside the culture of a department that believed in growth, mentoring, and visible excellence, they became part of something bigger. Students watched each other rise. Faculty expected effort and rewarded seriousness. Community and rigor were not opposites. They were partners.

By the end of Friday, Malik and Nia met on the lawn with matching exhaustion and different kinds of satisfaction.

“How’d office hours go?” Nia asked.

Malik adjusted the strap of his backpack. “Useful. Actually useful. I think I’ve been trying to solve some problems before I really understand what kind of problem they are.”

Nia grinned. “Welcome to the human experience.”

“And you?”

“I helped Keisha stop being afraid of the notation.” Nia lifted one shoulder. “Which might also be me helping myself stop being afraid of my own messy notes.”

Malik smirked. “Your notes are not notes. They’re weather systems.”

“They are concept maps.”

“They are storms with arrows.”

She pointed at him. “And yet they work.”

He could not argue with that.

Saturday transformed the campus entirely.

By noon, the energy around FAMU had become impossible to ignore. Albany State was in town, and the football game had poured excitement into every visible corner of student life. The walkways gleamed with school colors. Music rolled across the grounds in waves. Friends called to one another from across the quad. Vendors, alumni, and students moved through the day with the familiar confidence of a campus that knew how to celebrate itself.

For Malik, game day had once felt like a distraction to manage. This year, or at least this week, he was beginning to understand something subtler: balance did not mean rejecting joy. It meant learning how to carry purpose through joy without losing either.

He sat at an outdoor table near the student center before the larger crowds fully took over, tablet open, refining his internship and career checklist. Resume bullets. Research interests. Long-term actuarial benchmarks. It was not glamorous work, but it steadied him. Every line he clarified reminded him that this first semester was not an isolated test of intelligence. It was a foundation.

Dr. Bennett’s voice echoed in his head as he revised a line describing his academic interests: *Interpret numbers as evidence.*

Evidence of skill.
Evidence of discipline.
Evidence of promise.

He looked up when Nia arrived carrying an iced drink and enough game-day enthusiasm for both of them.

“You are the only person on this campus who can make a football Saturday look like a board meeting,” she said.

“It’s not a board meeting,” Malik replied. “It’s a strategic adjustment window.”

“That is somehow worse.”

But she sat anyway.

Nia had just come from a student organization planning meeting, one of several spaces where her energy made immediate sense. She was the kind of person who could care deeply about mathematics and still move naturally through the social ecosystem of campus life. She was learning, however, that leadership could not mean saying yes to every meeting, every request, every possibility. Even in her excitement, there was more selectivity now. More intention.

“How was the meeting?” Malik asked.

“Good,” she said. “Productive. Not chaotic. Which honestly feels like a miracle.” She paused. “They want to do more with freshman involvement this year. More study support, more visible encouragement, more reasons for people not to feel isolated in hard classes.”

“That sounds like you.”

She looked at him. “What’s that supposed to mean?”

“It means,” Malik said evenly, “you make difficult things feel possible. That seems useful.”

The compliment landed with enough sincerity that she did not joke it away.

By late afternoon, the game-day atmosphere had fully swelled. They let themselves be part of it—walking through the crowd, catching pieces of conversation, hearing the pulse of the band, feeling the bright collective pride that made FAMU feel larger than any one classroom or any one week’s stress. Yet even as they smiled and moved with the current of campus life, an undercurrent remained between them.

Grades were still coming.
And beyond grades, that research flyer still waited.

Sunday restored a quieter rhythm.

Homework was due. Laundry hummed in dorm basements. The campus, while not silent, felt more honest. Less performance, more preparation.

In the library, Malik disappeared into a two-hour deep-work block with the seriousness of someone honoring a pact with himself. He chose a table far from the entrance, arranged his materials with geometric neatness, wrote the date at the top of the page, and began. No multitasking. No checking notifications. No rushing because the first part felt easy.

Deep work, he was learning, was not just a study habit. It was a form of self-respect.

Across the building, Nia led a whiteboard study circle that was growing into a real community. Not a random cluster of stressed students. A functioning academic circle. She opened the session by writing one sentence in large letters:

**Explain before solve.**

“Anybody can follow steps they just saw ten seconds ago,” she told the group. “What we need is transfer. If the problem changes shape, your brain can’t panic. So say what’s happening first.”

David was there again. Keisha too. This time they spoke sooner. Asked more. Laughed even. The room carried that rare kind of momentum where nobody wanted to look foolish because everyone was too busy trying to improve.

When Malik joined the group later, he noticed something immediately.

“They’re sharper,” he said quietly to Nia while the others packed up.

She smiled, tired but proud. “So are we.”

Monday brought another calculus problem set, and with it the familiar threat of overload. But this time the strain did not feel directionless. Malik used a timer for his drills, not to race, but to study how pressure changed his thinking. Nia rewrote a full lecture’s worth of notes into a concept map so clean it almost looked like art. Dr. Brooks reminded the class to show their reasoning clearly, and for once the instruction no longer felt like criticism in advance. It felt like a standard they were starting to meet.

Tuesday layered more refinement onto the week. Malik returned to office hours, this time with narrower questions and greater confidence. Nia helped another peer over a conceptual hump and realized she was slowly becoming the person younger students sought out on instinct. Wednesday’s quiz and concept check window tightened everybody’s nerves again, but not in the same way as before. The fear was still there. So was the adrenaline. Yet now those feelings had structure around them.

By Thursday, the emotional shape of the week finally sharpened into something unmistakable.

Some midterm results had started to circulate.
Not all.
Just enough.

A classmate stepped out into the hallway with a grin too bright to hide. Another looked dazed. Another groaned and promised a total life reset by next Monday. The department was alive with speculation, whispered comparisons, fragile confidence, and private dread.

Malik and Nia sat together after their final study session of the week in a corridor washed gold by the late-afternoon sun. The building had begun to empty, though not completely. Somewhere down the hall, a professor’s voice rose and fell behind a half-closed office door. A vending machine buzzed softly. The moment felt suspended between outcomes.

“Have you checked?” Nia asked.

Malik looked at his phone, then turned it face down on the bench between them. “No.”

Nia blinked. “You? Not checking immediately?”

“I’m trying something new.”

“That sounds dangerous.”

He let out a small laugh. “I want to be steady before I see whatever is there.”

Nia leaned back against the wall. “Fair.” Then, after a beat: “I checked one of mine. Not all of them.”

“And?”

She made a face that was half relief, half disbelief. “Good enough to breathe. Not good enough to get arrogant.”

“That might be the most useful grading category I’ve ever heard.”

They sat with that for a moment.

Then Nia nudged the folded flyer sticking out of Malik’s notebook. “So. Are we pretending not to think about that too?”

Malik pulled out the FAMU Undergraduate Research Symposium flyer and unfolded it carefully. Even now it seemed to carry a different kind of voltage than the graded work around them. Exams measured what they had learned so far. Research hinted at what they might become.

“I’m not pretending,” he said. “I’m calculating.”

Nia smiled. “Of course you are.”

He looked down at the flyer, then back up at her. “Last week was survival. This week feels like a verdict. But I don’t think the real question is whether we did well enough to feel safe.”

Nia’s expression shifted, growing more focused.

“What’s the real question?” she asked.

Malik answered slowly, as if hearing the truth of it while he spoke. “Whether we’re ready to do something bigger than just keeping up.”

The hallway seemed to quiet around them.

Nia took the flyer from his hand and smoothed it across her knee. “Then maybe next week we stop talking about research like it belongs to older students.”

Malik looked at her, and for the first time the possibility did not feel like a fantasy or an overreach. It felt difficult. Serious. Earned enough to attempt.

Not guaranteed.
But real.

Through the open windows at the end of the corridor, the last light of day spread across campus. Students moved between buildings with backpacks, laughter, fatigue, ambition, and all the ordinary invisible burdens of becoming themselves. FAMU seemed to hold all of it at once—the expectation, the support, the pressure, the pride. The mathematics department was not merely testing them. It was shaping them.

Nia folded the flyer once more and handed it back.

“Okay,” she said, her voice bright with challenge again. “Next week, we need more than good instincts. We need a real idea.”

Malik nodded. “And probably faculty willing to tell us why our first idea isn’t good enough.”

“Even better.”

He stood, and she stood with him.

Neither of them checked their phones right away.

The unresolved tension of the week remained exactly where it belonged—alive, productive, and pointed forward. Grades were coming. Confidence was still provisional. The future had not opened for them yet, not fully. But it had cracked wide enough to let in a dangerous, exhilarating thought:

They might be ready for more than survival.

And if that thought proved true, the beginning of next week would demand courage of an entirely different kind.

---

# Week Eight: The Week of Steady Building

Friday morning began with the kind of relief that did not arrive loudly.

It came in a small breath.
A quick glance.
A phone screen lit beneath fluorescent hallway lights.
A number that did not solve everything, but settled something.

Malik Baptiste stood just outside Jackson Davis Hall with his backpack over one shoulder and finally checked the grade he had refused to open the evening before. Students moved around him in uneven currents, some walking fast to class, some lingering in little circles of conversation, some pretending not to care while clearly caring a great deal. The campus was awake in that particular FAMU way—warm sunlight already spreading across brick and grass, voices rising from the walkway, banners lifting in a soft breeze—but Malik heard almost none of it for the first second after the grade loaded.

Then he exhaled.

Not perfect.
Not disappointing.
Strong.
Strong enough to confirm that the long nights, the targeted questions, the mistake logs in the margins of his notebook, and the deliberate slowing down had all amounted to something real.

He stared one moment longer, not out of vanity, but out of disbelief that steadiness could feel this satisfying.

“Good?” a voice asked.

He looked up to find Nia Reynolds approaching with a coffee cup in one hand and an expression already halfway to a smirk.

Malik locked his phone and nodded once. “Good enough to justify my personality.”

Nia laughed. “That sounds exactly like you.” She lifted her own phone. “Mine too. Not dramatic. Just… reassuring.”

They fell into step together toward class, and for a few moments neither of them said much. That silence was not awkward. It was earned. Last week’s tension had not vanished, but it had changed its nature. The question was no longer whether they could survive college mathematics at FAMU. The evidence, imperfect but persuasive, said yes.

Now the question was what to build on top of survival.

By the time they reached the building entrance, Nia tapped the folded research symposium flyer peeking out from Malik’s notebook.

“So,” she said, lowering her voice just enough to make it feel serious, “are we actually going to do something with that, or are we just carrying it around like a symbol of future greatness?”

Malik glanced at it. “I think this week we stop being hypothetical.”

Nia’s grin sharpened. “Now that is the most exciting thing you’ve said before 9 a.m.”

Inside, the day unfolded without spectacle, which turned out to be exactly what the week needed.

There was no football-game spike, no all-campus disruption, no crisis pressing down from every direction. Instead there was the subtler challenge of ordinary discipline: the kind of week that reveals whether growth is real because nobody is forcing it into visibility.

Later that morning, Malik sat in a side room near office hours with three pages of targeted calculus questions. Not panicked questions. Not “help me because everything is broken” questions. Focused ones. He had marked each problem with colored tabs: where his timing slipped, where his assumptions got muddy, where he could arrive at a correct answer while still feeling conceptually late.

Dr. Price reviewed one of Malik’s pages, then leaned back in his chair.

“You’ve improved your setup,” he said.

Malik tried not to sound too eager. “I’ve been working on that.”

“I can tell. But here”—Dr. Price tapped the middle of a solution—“you are still treating the model like the answer is already obvious. State assumptions before modeling. If you don’t name the world your math is describing, you can make elegant mistakes very quickly.”

Malik nodded and wrote the sentence down nearly word for word.

Dr. Price stood and moved to the board. “Anyone can manipulate symbols. Strong mathematicians and analysts learn to say what must be true before the symbols begin to move. That habit will help you in calculus, statistics, actuarial work—everywhere.”

The conversation lasted less than twenty minutes, but Malik came away from it with the pleasant discomfort of someone discovering that improvement had layers. Getting better did not mean being finished. It meant being capable of seeing finer weaknesses.

Outside, under a shaded walkway lined with students balancing notebooks, snacks, and half-finished conversations, Nia was doing what had slowly become one of her roles in the department without anybody formally assigning it to her.

A sophomore named Alana had caught her after class with a question that had started as one difficult concept and widened into a more familiar frustration.

“I swear I understand it when the professor says it,” Alana admitted, pressing her pen against her notebook. “Then I get back to my room and it all turns into fragments.”

Nia nodded with immediate recognition. “That means the idea hasn’t connected into a picture yet.”

“A picture?”

“Not literally. Well—sometimes literally.” Nia flipped to a blank page. “You can’t always hold disconnected steps in your head. You need a shape. A map. Some reason one part belongs next to another.”

She began sketching arrows, labels, and short phrases rather than equations alone. Her notes always looked a little alive, as if the ideas were in motion rather than pinned down. Students who first saw them sometimes thought they were too messy. Students who learned from them understood quickly that the mess had structure.

Alana watched, then leaned in. “Oh. Wait. So this part isn’t just another step. It’s the reason the next method works.”

“Exactly.”

“That would’ve been nice to know yesterday.”

Nia smiled. “Yesterday was the rough draft. Today is the better version.”

That, too, felt like FAMU.

The campus did not only reward polished brilliance. In the mathematics department especially, there was a culture of visible growth, of office doors opening, of upperclassmen advising first-years, of professors expecting more because they believed students could rise to it. Undergraduate research posters from previous years hung in hallways like quiet promises. Announcements about seminars, tutoring, and student organizations layered the bulletin boards. The message was constant, even when unspoken: you do not have to arrive finished to belong here.

Saturday carried that spirit into a slower, steadier campus rhythm.

Malik spent part of the morning on the lawn near the student center with his internship and career checklist open again, but this time it looked less like a private survival document and more like a blueprint. He updated a section on long-term goals, adding notes about actuarial pathways, programming skills to strengthen, and something new: possible research interests that connected mathematics to real-world modeling.

When Nia found him there, she dropped into the seat across from him and squinted at the tablet.

“Please tell me that isn’t another five-year plan.”

“It’s not,” Malik said.

She narrowed her eyes. “Is it a three-year plan pretending to be flexible?”

He gave her a look. “It’s an updated checklist.”

“That answer was somehow even more suspicious.”

But she was teasing lightly, and he knew it. She had spent her own morning in a student organization planning meeting, talking through freshman engagement ideas, peer support, and how to make more students feel invited into academic life rather than intimidated by it.

“They want more programming that connects first-years to opportunities earlier,” she said. “Not just social events. Real pathways. Research talks, peer panels, study support, faculty introductions.”

Malik looked up. “That sounds smart.”

“It does. And honestly, it feels necessary. Too many students think undergraduate research is something you hear about after everyone else already knows the rules.”

There it was again—that same thread from last week, no longer abstract.

Malik turned the tablet toward her and showed a new section he had typed: **Questions about research**.

Nia’s expression changed instantly. “You actually made a section for it.”

“I said we should stop being hypothetical.”

She leaned back, pleased in a way she did not bother hiding. “Okay. I’m impressed.”

“Document that. It may not happen again.”

They spent the next half hour not committing to a research plan exactly, but giving their curiosity structure. What kind of topics interested them? Which professors might be approachable? How much did first-years need to know before asking serious questions? What counted as a good question versus a vague ambition? Their discussion had the texture of early possibility—still clumsy in places, but no longer imaginary.

Sunday brought the weekly reset.

Homework was due. Dorm hallways filled with laundry baskets, review sheets, and people trying to reclaim a few hours before Monday arrived. In the library, Malik protected a two-hour deep-work block with near-religious seriousness. He chose a quiet table, silenced his phone, set a timer, and worked calculus problems not for speed alone, but for rhythm. Each time he felt himself rushing, he stopped and restated the assumptions. Each time he felt panic begin to push him toward shortcuts, he reset the setup first.

Across campus, Nia led her whiteboard study circle again, and the group had grown.

Not dramatically.
But noticeably.

Keisha was back. David too. Alana arrived with two friends. Somebody brought extra markers. Somebody else brought snacks. What could have become a chaotic group instead held together because Nia had begun shaping it with intention.

“Same rule as last week,” she said, writing in bold strokes across the board: **Explain before solve.**

A few students groaned in playful protest.

“I know,” Nia said. “Terrible burden. Understanding your own logic.”

Laughter loosened the room, and then the work started.

She moved from problem to problem with a style that never felt showy. She asked questions before offering solutions. She turned silence into participation. When someone gave a partial answer, she treated it like a foundation instead of a failure. And by the end of the session, the room carried that same unmistakable energy as before: people were not merely getting through assignments. They were learning how to think together.

When Malik arrived near the end and watched from the doorway for a moment, he saw what others were starting to see. Nia was not just a strong student. She was becoming a mentor.

Afterward, as they walked out into the late afternoon light, he said it plainly.

“You know they trust you.”

Nia glanced at him. “That sounds heavy.”

“It is.”

She was quiet for a moment after that. “I’m trying to deserve it.”

“You do.”

Monday and Tuesday deepened the week’s central lesson: growth is often quiet before it becomes obvious.

A new calculus problem set arrived. Malik practiced timed drills again, but now the timer no longer felt like an enemy. It felt like a diagnostic tool. Nia rewrote another dense page of notes into a concept map that connected theorem, intuition, and application in a way that made the chapter feel less like a wall and more like a path. Dr. Bennett reminded a class that numbers were evidence, not decoration. Dr. Brooks repeated his steady refrain about showing reasoning clearly. In office hours, Dr. Price once again returned to assumptions, to setup, to discipline before cleverness.

The repetition mattered.

These were not random lines from different professors. They were beginning to sound like one conversation told in different voices.

Wednesday’s concept check window tightened the atmosphere slightly, but not in the way earlier quizzes had. The fear was still there, because college mathematics at this level did not stop being serious simply because students became more capable. But there was less chaos inside the fear now. More method. More recovery skill.

Nia helped another peer after class and noticed that she no longer had to force confidence into her voice. Malik walked into office hours already knowing how to ask sharper questions. Small things. Invisible things, if you were not paying attention. But those were often the things that changed a semester.

By Thursday, the week seemed at first to be ending with nothing dramatic at all.

And maybe that was the drama.

Late that afternoon, Malik and Nia sat on a bench outside the mathematics building while the campus eased toward evening. The light had softened. Students crossed the lawn in groups, trading jokes, carrying instrument cases, backpacks, and half-finished drinks. Somewhere in the distance, music lifted and fell. FAMU felt expansive in that hour—not only a place of classes, but of pathways, performances, ambitions, and futures unfolding in parallel.

Nia had the research flyer again, now more worn around the fold lines.

“We need an actual next step,” she said.

Malik nodded. “Agreed.”

“No dramatic speeches. No ‘someday we should.’ A real next step.”

He thought for a moment. “We each talk to one professor next week. Not asking for a full research position. Just asking informed questions.”

Nia tilted her head. “About undergraduate research? About how students get started?”

“About how first-years can prepare to contribute. About what kind of habits matter. About what we don’t know yet.”

A smile spread across her face slowly, the kind that meant a possibility had shifted into a plan.

“That’s good,” she said. “That’s actually good.”

Malik looked at her. “You sound surprised.”

“I am surprised. I expected your first suggestion to involve a spreadsheet.”

“It can still involve a spreadsheet.”

She laughed and shook her head. Then her expression softened.

“You know what I like about this?” she asked.

“What?”

“It doesn’t pretend we’re already ready. It just refuses to let that stop us.”

The words settled between them with more force than either expected.

That was the whole week, really. Not triumph. Not a collapse. Not a cinematic breakthrough. Something steadier, and maybe harder to earn: a week of proof that routines were becoming character, that the mathematics department’s expectations were shaping them rather than scaring them off, that FAMU’s culture of mentorship and student opportunity was not some distant thing happening to other people.

The sun lowered further, catching the edges of campus buildings in gold.

Students passed by wearing FAMU colors, laughing, planning, hurrying, dreaming. Posters for student organizations and academic events fluttered on nearby boards. Somewhere inside the building behind them, a professor was still talking through a problem with a student who had stayed late to ask. The whole place felt alive with effort and possibility.

Nia folded the flyer carefully and handed it back to Malik.

“Next week,” she said, “we ask.”

Malik took the flyer and slipped it into his notebook with unusual care.

“Next week,” he agreed.

It was a small promise.
But it carried real voltage.

Because this week had taught them that not every important turning point arrives in crisis. Some arrive in ordinary days, in repeated habits, in clearer questions, in the courage to move from private ambition to spoken intention.

They stood and started down the walkway together as evening gathered over FAMU.

Behind them, the mathematics building glowed with the last light.
Ahead of them, the next week waited with something sharper than anxiety and more daring than relief.

A question was about to leave the safety of their notebooks and enter the world.

And once they asked it, the year might begin opening in an entirely new way.

---

# Week Nine: The Week the Question Left the Notebook

Friday morning arrived carrying the unfinished sentence from the week before.

**Next week, we ask.**

For Malik Baptiste, the promise had followed him into sleep and back out again.
Not as fear exactly.
Not even as doubt.
More like a live wire tucked beneath ordinary routine.

He felt it while walking across campus with his backpack settled squarely between his shoulders and a pencil tucked behind one ear. He felt it while passing students laughing on the way to early classes, while stepping around a pair of freshmen taking photos near a row of rattler-green banners, while glancing up at a sky so bright and open it made the whole campus feel bigger than it had on the day he arrived.

FAMU had a way of doing that.
On some mornings, it felt like a collection of buildings and deadlines.
On others, it felt like a living invitation.
A place humming with effort, talent, culture, and possibility, as if every sidewalk held at least three different futures crossing paths at once.

This morning, it felt like both.

Malik reached the library before many of the loudest weekend currents had gathered. He liked that hour. The quiet in academic buildings before the day fully opened always made him feel as if the campus were breathing in before speaking. He chose a table near a window, spread out his notebook, problem set, and timer, and began his two-hour deep-work block with the seriousness of someone protecting not just time, but identity.

He had not always known how to study this way.
Or rather, he had always known how to work hard, but college mathematics was teaching him the difference between effort and structure. Hard work without rhythm could become panic in nicer clothes. He had learned that already. So now he began with a clean page and wrote, at the top, in block letters:

**SETUP BEFORE SPEED.**

Underneath it he wrote something Dr. Delgado had repeated enough times to become part of the architecture of his thinking:

**Clean setup before solving.**

He stared at the first problem for a full minute before moving his pencil.
Not because he was stuck.
Because he was trying to see the shape of the problem before he let himself touch the mechanics.

When the pencil finally moved, it moved more slowly than the Malik of three weeks ago would have tolerated. He named variables. He wrote assumptions. He noted what the question was actually asking. He forced himself to stay at the front end of the work until the structure felt honest.

By the time he reached the third page, the old urge to rush began tapping at him.
He could feel it in his shoulders.
The little internal pressure that whispered: *You know enough. Just go. Just finish. Just prove you can do it quickly.*

Instead, he stopped, sat back, and reset.

Around him, the library carried the small sounds of disciplined student life—pages turning, laptop keys clicking, a muffled cough, a chair sliding softly across tile. Near the far wall, two upperclassmen were quietly debating a statistics assignment. On another floor, somebody laughed and was immediately hushed. A student in a FAMU hoodie hurried past the window outside with a coffee balanced dangerously in one hand and a stack of folders in the other.

The whole place felt like evidence.
Evidence that work mattered here.
Evidence that striving did not make you strange.
Evidence that one of the deepest forms of campus life was not spectacle, but shared concentration.

When Malik finally packed up, he had completed fewer problems than he would have in a speed sprint.
But he trusted what he had done more.
That mattered.

Outside the library, under a stretch of shade where students often paused between classes, Nia Reynolds had already turned a rolling whiteboard into the center of a small temporary orbit.

Her study circle had started as a practical solution.
A few friends. A few markers. A place to untangle difficult ideas before they became crises.

Now it was becoming something a little more recognizable.
Not formal enough to be an organization.
Not casual enough to be random.
Somewhere in between, where useful things often begin.

“Okay,” Nia said, uncapping a marker with her teeth before catching herself and laughing. “Terrible habit. Ignore that part. The important part is the board.”

A few students laughed.
Keisha was there again, leaning forward with crossed arms and a look that always suggested she was ready to challenge weak explanations on principle. David sat with a notebook already open. Alana had arrived early this time and brought another student from her residence hall, a quiet first-year named Tori who admitted within thirty seconds that she was “not even sure what question to ask yet.”

Nia pointed at her with the marker like that was the best possible opening statement.

“That,” she said, “is a very respectable academic beginning.”

Tori blinked. “It is?”

“It means your confusion is honest. Honest confusion is workable. Fake confidence wastes everybody’s time.”

That got another laugh, but it also loosened the room. Nia had become good at that: reducing the emotional temperature just enough for people to start thinking clearly again.

She wrote the day’s guiding line across the whiteboard:

**If you can picture it, you can organize it.**

“Dr. Okafor says visualize the concept first,” she continued. “So before we grind through procedures, tell me what the idea is doing. Motion? Accumulation? Change? Approximation? What is the math trying to describe?”

The session unfolded with the kind of momentum that would have translated beautifully into comic panels: markers flashing across the board, students leaning in, eyebrows lifting when an explanation landed, somebody pointing with both hands at a diagram as if clarity itself deserved applause.

Nia did not dominate the board so much as conduct it.
She pulled answers out of hesitant students.
She translated abstract language into metaphors without making it childish.
She let silence breathe long enough for someone else to try.
And when Tori finally offered a shaky explanation that linked the concept map to the actual problem structure, Nia lit up with immediate delight.

“Yes,” she said. “Yes, that’s it. That is exactly the bridge. Say that again, slower.”

Tori did.
This time with more confidence.

And the others started building from there.

By the end of the session, what had begun as scattered uncertainty had tightened into shared understanding. Not perfect understanding. But real progress. The kind students could carry back to their rooms instead of leaving behind in the classroom.

When Malik arrived near the end, he did not interrupt. He stood near the doorway and watched Nia move the group from one idea to the next with that particular blend of patience and spark that seemed to belong to her naturally and yet had clearly been sharpened by experience.

When it wrapped, he helped wipe the board clean.

“You should start charging tuition,” he said.

Nia snorted. “Please. None of us can afford me.”

“I’m serious. This is becoming a whole thing.”

She glanced at the fading blue and green marker traces still ghosting the board. “Maybe. I just don’t want people feeling alone with it.”

Malik nodded once. He understood that more than he wanted to say out loud.

Then Nia straightened and gave him a sideways look.

“You ready for tomorrow?”

He knew she meant more than the football game.

“Not fully,” he said.

“Good. Full readiness is suspicious.”

Saturday woke the whole campus differently.

Before noon, the air around FAMU had already changed shape. The sidewalks carried more color, more sound, more anticipation. Tailgate energy began gathering in parking lots and open stretches near the stadium. Cars rolled in flying orange and green. Vendors called out over the low thump of music. Families in coordinated shirts moved in clusters, carrying folding chairs, coolers, and enough food to imply they were prepared for a full-scale celebration rather than a single game.

Game day at FAMU was never just a game.
It was rhythm.
Memory.
Community.
A performance of pride that spilled beyond the stadium and into the whole body of campus life.

And this week, against Alabama State, the energy felt especially bright.

Nia loved game-day atmosphere in a way she never apologized for. She loved the sound of the marching band warming up in fragments that already hinted at greatness. She loved the alumni stories that floated through the air with the smoke from grills. She loved seeing younger students realize, sometimes all at once, that college was not merely classes and stress, but belonging to something larger than themselves.

But she also knew what this semester had already taught them: joy needed structure too.

So when she met Malik outside the student center that morning, she was carrying a concept map in one hand and a small clear stadium bag in the other.

He looked at both and said, “That feels incredibly on-brand.”

“It’s called balance,” she said.

“It looks like advanced multitasking.”

“It looks like I’m enjoying game day *and* protecting my future. Revolutionary idea.”

He raised his hands. “I support the revolution.”

They claimed an outdoor table for ninety minutes before heading toward the louder parts of campus. Malik set a timer for his calculus drills; Nia rewrote one more page of notes into cleaner conceptual form. Around them, the social energy kept building—students calling to one another across the courtyard, somebody testing speakers, a cluster of first-years taking photos with handmade signs, a pair of student ambassadors directing visitors toward the right entrance.

Every few minutes, distraction brushed past them in visible form.

But they kept working.

Not grimly.
Not with resentment.
Just intentionally.

At one point Nia looked up, hearing the band more clearly now, and smiled almost helplessly. “Okay,” she said, pressing her palm to the notes as if physically holding herself down, “in exactly fifteen minutes I stop being noble.”

Malik checked his timer. “Twelve, actually.”

She stared at him. “I need you to know that this is why people think actuarial science majors are built in labs.”

He almost smiled. “And yet you continue to trust me with timekeeping.”

“Against my better judgment.”

When they finally joined the flow moving toward the game-day festivities, they did so without the private guilt that had shadowed earlier campus distractions. The work had been done. The morning had been protected. They had earned the exhale.

That changed everything.

The afternoon unfolded in a cascade of color and motion.
The Marching “100” sent sound across the air with the kind of authority that made conversations pause mid-sentence. The crowd surged and answered and sang. Children on their parents’ shoulders waved small rattler flags with total seriousness. Alumni greeted one another with the warm force of old family. Students moved through the scene with an ease that said they were still new enough to be impressed and already proud enough to feel at home.

Malik, who usually experienced public excitement with one part enjoyment and one part observational distance, found himself relaxing into it more than expected.

“This,” he said at one point, scanning the sea of green and orange, “is what the brochures cannot explain.”

Nia grinned. “Exactly.”

Because there was no brochure version of this.
No polished sentence that could fully translate what it meant to study at a place where academic rigor and cultural life did not compete for importance, but reinforced each other. Where the same students yelling themselves hoarse in the stands would be back in classrooms, labs, tutoring centers, office hours, and planning meetings within forty-eight hours. Where school spirit and intellectual ambition were not opposites.

At FAMU, they were part of the same story.

By evening, both of them were tired in the satisfying way that follows a full day honestly lived. But even as the stadium lights dimmed and the campus began easing back toward normal rhythm, the unresolved thread from Week Eight returned.

Tomorrow, or soon after, they needed to stop circling the idea of research and actually step toward it.

That awareness followed them into Sunday.

Sunday at FAMU had its own texture: less performative than Saturday, more practical, but still full of life. Residence halls hummed with reset energy. Laundry machines worked overtime. Students shuffled through lounges carrying laptops, chargers, notebooks, snacks, and the expression of people trying to build order before Monday arrived. Somewhere, somebody was definitely promising themselves a new semester strategy again. Somewhere else, somebody was already behind and pretending not to know it.

Malik spent part of the afternoon in office hours with a list of targeted questions that had grown more precise over the past few weeks. Dr. Bennett sat across from him, reviewing a section of work that mixed numerical results with written interpretation.

“You’re getting cleaner technically,” Dr. Bennett said, handing the page back. “Now tell me what the numbers *mean.*”

Malik frowned slightly. “I thought I did.”

“You described them.” Dr. Bennett tapped a line with his pen. “I’m asking for interpretation. Numbers are evidence. Evidence only matters when you can say what it supports.”

Malik looked down again.
That landed.

Dr. Bennett leaned back in his chair. “Anybody can produce output. Analysts, actuaries, researchers—people who become valuable in the world—learn how to connect the result to the decision. What does this result suggest? What does it rule out? Why should anyone care?”

The conversation drifted naturally from course material into something larger.
Into habits.
Into professional identity.
Into what mathematically strong students needed to practice if they wanted to become more than good test-takers.

Malik hesitated only briefly before asking the question that had been waiting for its moment.

“Can I ask something a little outside the assignment?”

Dr. Bennett gave him a look that was calm, curious, and open all at once. “Those are often the useful questions.”

Malik felt the wire of nerves again, but this time he did not retreat from it.

“I’ve been thinking about undergraduate research,” he said. “Not because I think I’m ready to do something advanced tomorrow. More because I want to understand how students start preparing early. Especially students interested in modeling, analytics, actuarial work… things that connect mathematics to decisions.”

For a brief second, he worried the question might sound naive.

Instead, Dr. Bennett smiled.
Not indulgently.
Genuinely.

“That,” he said, “is exactly the right kind of first-year question.”

Something in Malik’s chest eased.

Dr. Bennett went on. “Too many students think research is a secret door you discover late. It usually starts much more simply than that. Curiosity. Reliability. Willingness to read what you don’t yet fully understand. Asking better questions over time. And showing faculty that if they open a door, you’ll walk through it responsibly.”

Malik nodded so quickly he almost looked startled by his own relief.

They spent the next several minutes talking through possibilities: faculty projects, independent reading, statistics applications, what undergraduate research could look like in math versus applied settings, how students could position themselves through habits before formal opportunities even appeared.

By the end, Malik had a page of notes that looked less like a fantasy and more like a map.

And more than that, he had a first answer to the unspoken fear beneath the question.

No, he was not foolish for asking early.
Yes, early was in fact the point.

Elsewhere on campus, Nia was living a parallel version of the same breakthrough.

She had spent part of the afternoon helping Alana sort through a conceptual snag that had become, as these things often did, half mathematics and half morale.

“I think I keep waiting to feel ‘caught up,’” Alana admitted as they sat in a quiet corner with notebooks spread between them. “And when I don’t, I act like I’m failing.”

Nia capped her pen. “You may need a crueler definition of caught up.”

Alana laughed despite herself. “Crueler?”

“More realistic,” Nia said. “In math, ‘caught up’ sometimes just means you know where the confusion is and you’re facing it on purpose.”

They worked another fifteen minutes before the concept finally clicked.
Alana’s shoulders dropped in visible relief.

“You should teach,” she said.

Nia made a face. “Everybody says that when they’re grateful. It’s dangerous. I might start believing it.”

“I’m serious.”

Nia smiled, but the comment stayed with her after Alana left.
Because under the teasing, it touched something real.
She did not yet know exactly what shape her future would take—pure mathematics, teaching, research, some combination she could not yet name—but she was beginning to recognize that explaining ideas was not just something she happened to be good at. It was part of how she belonged.

Monday deepened the research thread instead of letting it fade.

A new calculus problem set arrived, as problem sets always did, with impeccable timing and no respect for anyone’s emotional narrative. Malik updated his internship and career checklist between classes, but now he added a fresh section with more confidence than before:

**Research Preparation**
- Ask about reading opportunities
- Strengthen written interpretation of results
- Track interests: modeling, statistics, actuarial applications
- Build faculty relationships early

He stared at the list for a second after writing it.
It looked modest.
But it felt like proof.

That afternoon, Nia attended a student organization planning meeting where the conversation turned toward academic programming for underclassmen. The room buzzed with suggestions: peer panels, tutoring resources, study-break socials, faculty mixers, career talks, time-management workshops.

At one point, a senior named Janelle said, “We keep talking like first-years need confidence first and information second. Sometimes it’s the opposite. Sometimes access to good information *creates* confidence.”

Nia sat up straighter.
That felt true immediately.

When there was a pause in the discussion, she added, “And maybe more visibility around undergraduate research too. Not in a scary, elite way. In a ‘here’s how students actually get started’ way. Because a lot of people probably assume it’s for somebody else.”

Heads nodded around the table.
The idea stayed in the room.

That mattered.

By Tuesday, the week had taken on a quieter but unmistakable forward pull.

Malik returned to the library for another deep-work block, but this time his mind kept circling back not only to equations, but to what lay beyond them. He could feel his internal picture widening. Course success still mattered. Grades still mattered. Precision still mattered. But now they seemed attached to pathways instead of floating as isolated demands.

Nia led another whiteboard session, and this time the students arrived with more energy from the start. Someone had brought better markers. Someone else had brought index cards for anonymous questions. Tori, who had barely spoken on Friday, volunteered an explanation before being asked.

Nia noticed immediately.
So did everyone else.

“That,” Nia said, pointing at her with approval, “is called momentum.”

The room answered with quick applause against tabletops.
Tori covered her face, smiling.

This too was part of the FAMU mathematics culture they were beginning to understand from the inside: rigorous expectations, yes, but also visible encouragement. Growth was noticed here. It was named. It was strengthened through community.

Wednesday brought the concept-check pressure window again, and with it the familiar tightening of nerves across campus. But the mood had changed from the early weeks of the semester. Students were not calmer because the material had become easier. They were calmer because they had started building systems around the difficulty.

Malik practiced with a timer and forced himself to pause at the start of each problem long enough to identify what was being asked and what assumptions were in play. Nia rewrote one final page of notes into a visual sequence that made the logic easier to retain under stress.

When the two of them crossed paths that afternoon, standing in the hallway as students streamed around them, they had the look of people tired by serious work but steadied by it too.

“How’d it go?” Nia asked.

“Not perfect,” Malik said.

“That answer is becoming your brand.”

“It went better than it would have a month ago.”

She nodded once. “That’s the right metric.”

Then he asked, “Did you talk to anyone?”

Her eyes sharpened. She knew which question he meant.

“Not yet,” she admitted. “I’m waiting for the right moment.”

Malik gave her a look that was mild but unmistakably familiar now.

“That sounds suspiciously like stalling.”

Nia pressed a hand to her chest. “I cannot believe you would say something so accurate.”

He laughed.
A short laugh, but real.

Thursday finally gave her the opening.

After class, while a few students gathered their things and drifted toward the door, Dr. Okafor remained near the board answering one last conceptual question with his usual mix of rigor and imagination. He had a way of speaking about mathematics as if it were not just a discipline to master, but a landscape to enter. Even difficult material sounded less hostile when he explained it.

Nia waited until the room thinned out.
Then she walked down the aisle before she could overthink the choice.

“Dr. Okafor?”

He turned. “Yes, Ms. Reynolds?”

For one dangerous half-second, all the clean versions of the question vanished.
But then she remembered the promise from the bench last week.
Next week, we ask.

So she asked.

“I wanted to know more about how undergraduates get involved in research,” she said. “Especially early. I’m not asking because I think I’m already prepared for something huge. I just don’t want to wait until I’m ‘ready’ and realize I should have started learning how this works much sooner.”

A slow smile spread across his face.
It was the kind professors gave when a student had just asked the kind of question they hoped more students would ask.

“That is wise,” he said simply.

The single word steadied her.

He rested one hand lightly against the edge of a desk and continued. “Research begins before formal projects. It begins in attention. In learning to live with difficult questions long enough to sharpen them. In reading beyond assigned material. In conversations. In intellectual patience.”

Nia listened as if each sentence were a match lighting another part of the future.

Dr. Okafor went on to describe the department’s undergraduate research culture—not as a distant prestige track, but as an extension of the same habits they were already being taught to value. Ask good questions. Build strong foundations. Be dependable. Stay curious. Seek mentors. Learn how to persist when understanding does not arrive quickly.

He mentioned faculty who enjoyed working with motivated students. He spoke warmly about the department’s belief that undergraduates could contribute meaningfully when guided well. He pointed out past student presentations, conference experiences, and the ways research could deepen both confidence and identity.

“You do not have to arrive fully formed to begin,” he said. “In fact, one of the gifts of undergraduate research is that it helps form you.”

Nia felt that line settle into her with unusual force.

Before she left, he recommended a few practical first steps: attend talks, ask faculty what they are reading, keep a notebook of mathematical questions that persist beyond homework, and pay attention to the topics that keep pulling her back.

When she stepped outside afterward, the late-afternoon air felt charged.
Not because her life had transformed in a single conversation.
But because a door she had imagined as heavy and guarded had turned out to be real, reachable, and already a little open.

She found Malik on a bench outside the mathematics building, notebook open, as if the universe had staged the scene on purpose.

“Well?” he asked.

Nia sat down beside him and let herself smile first.
A full smile.
The kind that answered before words did.

“I asked.”

He closed the notebook immediately. “And?”

“And it was not ridiculous. It was, apparently, wise.”

His eyebrows lifted. “That sounds promising.”

“It was more than promising.” She looked out across the lawn, where students were crossing through the gold light with backpacks, instrument cases, and end-of-day fatigue. “It was clarifying.”

So they told each other everything.
Dr. Bennett’s language about evidence, interpretation, and early preparation.
Dr. Okafor’s language about attention, curiosity, and being formed by the work.
How both conversations, though different in style, had pointed toward the same truth: they did not need to wait for some magical future version of themselves to begin moving toward research.
They needed to begin preparing now, in ordinary ways, through habits that were already available.

For a while they sat in that realization without trying to perform it.
No big speeches.
No dramatic declarations.
Just the warm, electrified quiet of two students recognizing that the horizon had shifted.

Then Malik said, “We should write this down before it turns into inspiration and disappears.”

Nia laughed. “There’s the spreadsheet spirit.”

But she opened her notebook anyway.

Together they made a rough list:
- Keep asking faculty questions
- Attend department talks and events
- Track topics that stay interesting after class ends
- Practice explaining ideas clearly
- Build consistency, not just bursts of brilliance
- Look for ways to help and learn at the same time

By the time they finished, the sun had dropped lower, painting the buildings in amber and shadow.
Music drifted from somewhere farther down the walkway. A cluster of students in FAMU shirts headed toward an evening meeting. Near the entrance to the building, a professor held the door for three students still talking about class. The whole scene radiated what the week had quietly become about: not merely surviving college, but entering its deeper opportunities with intention.

Nia tapped the edge of her notebook.

“You know what this feels like?” she asked.

“What?”

“Like the start of something that won’t look dramatic to anybody else yet.”

Malik considered that, then nodded.

“Those are usually the real starts.”

The week could have ended there, with resolution neat enough to satisfy a chapter title.
But college life was rarely that tidy, and growth was even less so.

Because once the question left the notebook, it did not disappear.
It multiplied.
What would they read first?
Which professor would they ask next?
What kind of math actually pulled at them when no grade was attached?
Could they become the kind of students faculty trusted with serious opportunities?
Could curiosity survive the pressure of deadlines, quizzes, fatigue, and all the ordinary collisions of student life?

As evening settled over FAMU, those questions did not feel discouraging.
They felt alive.

And that aliveness carried its own tension.
A good tension.
A forward tension.
The sense that the year was beginning to ask more of them precisely because they had finally become ready to ask more of it.

When they stood to leave, neither rushed.
The mathematics building glowed behind them. Campus lights flickered on one by one. Somewhere in the distance, laughter lifted from a residence hall courtyard. Somewhere else, a study group was probably just beginning. The life of the university moved in every direction at once—scholarship, friendship, ambition, culture, pressure, possibility.

FAMU held all of it.
And increasingly, so did they.

As they started down the walkway, Nia tucked her notebook under one arm and said, “Next week we need a new question.”

Malik glanced at her. “Only one?”

She smiled. “Fine. Several. But better ones.”

He looked ahead into the deepening evening.
Into the next stretch of semester that no longer seemed like something happening *to* them, but something they were learning to enter on purpose.

“Good,” he said. “Because I think the year just got bigger.”

And it had.
Not through spectacle.
Not through a sudden miracle.
But through the simple brave act of asking early, listening carefully, and discovering that the future sometimes begins in a professor’s office, on a whiteboard full of half-erased ideas, or on a bench outside the mathematics building when two first-year students realize that belonging is no longer the question.

The question now was how far they were willing to grow.

That answer, still unresolved and bright with possibility, followed them into the coming week like the opening line of a chapter not yet written.

---

# Week Ten: The Week Pressure Learned Their Names

Friday began without fanfare, but both Malik Baptiste and Nia Reynolds could feel the campus tightening around the coming week.

Not in a dramatic way.
Not yet.

It was there in the quieter tone inside the library, in the way students spoke with half-finished confidence about “being ready,” in the sudden seriousness that came over even the most social corners of campus whenever somebody mentioned a quiz, a concept check, or the midterm-style exam window waiting just ahead.

FAMU still looked beautiful in the morning light. The breeze still moved through the trees. Students still crossed campus wearing Rattler green with coffee in hand, laughter rising in quick pockets between buildings. But underneath that ordinary movement was something else now: concentration. The kind that made a week feel important before it had even unfolded.

Malik felt it first as he sat at a table in his room with his internship and career checklist open beside a stack of calculus notes.

A month earlier, that pairing would have felt impossible. Career planning belonged to the future. Calculus urgency belonged to the present. He would have treated them as competing demands and resented both.

Now he understood something FAMU had been teaching him slowly, one demanding week at a time: serious goals did not wait politely for each other. You had to learn how to carry more than one kind of ambition at once.

He highlighted two items on the checklist, updated a deadline, then shut the page before it could steal too much mental space.

“Future Malik can be grateful,” he muttered. “Present Malik has derivatives to survive.”

Across campus, Nia was leaving a student organization planning meeting with her notebook pressed against her chest and three ideas still sparking at once in her mind. She loved that feeling. At FAMU, meetings did not just feel administrative. They often felt alive—full of students trying to build something worthwhile for each other. That mattered to her. It was one of the reasons she had been drawn to this place in the first place.

But as she stepped out into the hallway and glanced at the time, the familiar pressure returned.

Too many moving pieces.
Too many things she cared about.
Too many opportunities that seemed good enough to say yes to.

She stopped walking, leaned against the wall for a moment, and opened her notebook to a blank page.

At the top she wrote:

**What must matter most this week?**

Underneath, she listed three answers.
1. Prepare clearly.
2. Protect energy.
3. Show up for the people who are also trying.

It was not a perfect plan.
But it was a boundary.
And lately, boundaries were becoming one of the hidden forms of wisdom in her first-year life.

By Saturday, the pressure had become communal.

Malik’s two-hour library deep-work block began in silence and ended with his brain humming from the effort of staying disciplined. He was tempted, more than once, to race through problems just to feel productive. Instead, he forced himself to slow down, state each assumption, and write clean setups before solving. Dr. Price’s voice stayed with him even though the professor was nowhere nearby: *State assumptions before modeling.*

It sounded simple when professors said it.
In practice, it required honesty.

Malik noticed that the hardest problems were not always the ones with the most steps. Sometimes they were the ones that exposed how quickly he wanted to move past uncertainty without naming it.

Later that afternoon, he found Nia at a whiteboard study circle she had organized. Students stood in loose clusters, markers uncapped, notebooks open, everybody trying to turn private confusion into something workable.

Nia was in her element.

“Don’t just tell me the answer,” she said to a sophomore volunteer who had stepped in to help. “Tell us what made that the right first move.”

A few students laughed softly.

“No, seriously,” she said, smiling. “That’s where half the learning is. The first move tells the truth about what you think the problem is.”

Malik leaned against the wall for a moment and watched her work.

He admired this about her, though he did not always say it out loud. Nia could make academic effort feel shared instead of isolating. She did not flatten difficulty. She did not pretend everything was easy. But she had a gift for creating space where people could struggle without feeling small.

When the group thinned, she turned to him.

“You look like you’ve been negotiating with a limit problem for two hours.”

“Three, actually.”

“That bad?”

“That honest.”

She grinned. “That might be progress.”

He nodded toward the board. “You’re building a whole little math civilization out here.”

“Somebody has to.” She capped a marker. “Everybody’s tense. If we don’t make room to think together, people start panicking alone.”

That sentence stayed with him longer than he expected.

By Sunday, the weekly reset arrived. Homework was due. Notes had to be reorganized. Plans had to be made before the week made them instead.

Malik used a timer for calculus drills and hated how exposed it made him feel. The ticking clock transformed every hesitation into something visible. Each delay felt measurable.

But he also noticed something encouraging: he was not collapsing when the timer pressed him. He was learning how to remain himself under pressure.

Not perfectly.
But more often.

Nia spent part of the evening rewriting her notes into a concept map, turning scattered pages into structure. Arrows linked ideas across chapters. Definitions became relationships. Procedures became patterns.

Her roommate looked over from across the room and said, “That looks less like notes and more like detective work.”

Nia smiled without looking up. “That’s because math is detective work. People just hide that part under symbols.”

Monday brought a fresh problem set and the kind of seriousness that settles over campus before exam windows. The walkways were still full, the conversations still warm, but more students were carrying folders, review sheets, and the look of people rehearsing courage.

Malik went to office hours with targeted questions instead of vague worry. That alone felt like growth.

Dr. Delgado looked over his work, tapped the top of one page, and said, “Your setup is improving. Cleaner start, cleaner mind.”

Malik let out a breath he had not realized he was holding.

“So I’m not just getting slower?”

Dr. Delgado almost smiled. “You’re getting deliberate. Students confuse those two all the time.”

Across the hall later that day, Nia helped a classmate untangle a difficult concept by drawing it three different ways until it clicked. She did not rush the explanation. She kept returning to the image first, just as Dr. Okafor often encouraged: *Visualize the concept first.*

That was one of the quiet gifts of the department. The professors were not merely delivering content. They were shaping habits of mind. Again and again, FAMU’s mathematics culture showed itself not only through rigor, but through mentorship—the steady insistence that students could become deeper thinkers if given both challenge and guidance.

Tuesday finally arrived, and with it the first true edge of the week.

The midterm-style exam window was no longer approaching.
It was here.

Students moved through the morning with a heightened stillness. Voices dropped. Jokes came quicker and ended faster. Backpacks looked heavier, though nothing inside them had changed.

Malik updated one last career checklist item before class, almost as an act of defiance. He refused to let the exam make his whole identity collapse into one moment. Nia came from another planning meeting, but this time she kept it short and left early, choosing her priorities on purpose.

When they met outside the building, neither pretended to be relaxed.

“You ready?” Nia asked.

“No,” Malik said.

She blinked.

Then he added, “But I’m prepared. I think that’s the more important one.”

That made her laugh—a small, needed sound in a tense morning.

“Okay,” she said. “That was annoyingly wise.”

Inside, the exam did what real academic pressure always does: it revealed habits.

Malik felt the old urge to sprint when he recognized the first problem. This time he resisted. He wrote the setup first. He named the assumptions. He kept his breathing steady. One question later, when uncertainty hit, he did not let panic reinterpret it as failure. He moved, recalibrated, and kept going.

Nia, meanwhile, felt the danger from another direction. Her mind was quick enough to chase too many interpretations at once. She had to choose clarity over cleverness. More than once, she silently repeated to herself: *One problem at a time. One clean thought at a time.*

When they emerged, neither one was smiling immediately.

“How bad?” Malik asked.

Nia exhaled. “Not bad. Just intense. There were two questions that definitely wanted to start a fight.”

“That sounds right.”

They started walking, and slowly the tension cracked enough for reflection.

By Wednesday, the pressure doubled: a quiz or concept check window layered over the exam rhythm already in motion. It felt almost unfair.

Which was exactly why the week mattered.

Anyone could feel committed on a calm week.
This was the week that tested whether their new systems actually held.

Nia led another whiteboard session, but this one felt sharper than the Saturday circle. Less exploratory. More urgent. Students were tired, but they kept showing up for each other. Malik took a marker and worked through a problem in deliberate steps, hearing Dr. Bennett’s reminder in his head: *Interpret numbers as evidence.*

Not verdicts.
Evidence.

It changed the mood in the room.
A wrong answer became a clue.
A gap became something you could address.
Not something you had to hide.

By Thursday, exhaustion had settled into their shoulders, but so had something else.

Confidence.
Not the loud kind.
Not the kind that boasts before results arrive.

The earned kind.
The kind built from repeated choices made under strain.

Malik ran one final set of timed calculus drills and noticed, to his surprise, that the timer no longer sounded like an enemy. It sounded like a challenge he knew how to meet. Nia rewrote her final notes into a cleaner concept map and realized she could now see the week as a coherent story rather than a blur of threats.

Later that evening, they crossed a quieter part of campus together, the light softening around them, the buildings holding the last warmth of day.

FAMU did not feel easy.
It felt worth it.

That difference mattered.

“You know what’s strange?” Nia said.

“What?”

“A few weeks ago, a week like this would’ve made me feel like I was falling behind my own life.”

“And now?”

She looked toward the mathematics building before answering.

“Now it feels like I’m actually becoming the person this place keeps asking me to be.”

Malik was quiet for a moment.
Then he nodded.

“Yeah,” he said. “Me too.”

They stood there another second, neither hurrying to fill the silence.

In the distance, laughter drifted from another part of campus. Somewhere nearby, students were still studying. Somewhere else, somebody was probably already worrying about the next round of assignments, the next score, the next test of discipline.

And that was the truth waiting at the edge of the week: this was not the end of the pressure.
It was proof that they could survive it differently now.

Before they parted, Nia adjusted the strap on her bag and said, “Next week, when the results start coming back, we’re not spiraling.”

Malik raised an eyebrow. “That a request or a rule?”

“Both.”

He almost laughed. “Fine. But if one of those grades comes back ugly, I reserve the right to stare at a wall for five minutes.”

“Five,” she said. “Not fifty.”

“Deal.”

They walked in opposite directions then, carrying fatigue, discipline, and the unfinished tension of a week that had demanded a lot and still had not fully shown its hand.

Behind them, the campus kept breathing with its usual mixture of challenge and promise.
Ahead of them, the next week waited with answers they did not yet have.

And for the first time, that uncertainty felt less like a threat than the opening line of another chapter.

---

# Week Eleven: The Week the Tension Turned Into Motion

The promise they made at the end of the previous week lasted less than twelve hours before it was tested.

By Friday morning, the first ripples of result-season had begun moving quietly through campus.

Not official grades, not all at once, not the kind of dramatic reveal students imagine when they think about college pressure. It came the way so much of academic tension came at FAMU—in fragments. A comment after class. A number posted online. A text from a classmate that simply said, *Did you check yet?*

Malik saw that exact message while walking across campus beneath a bright October sky that looked far too calm for the kind of week he knew he was entering.

He stopped near a bench, stared at the screen, and did not open the portal.

Not yet.

He could still hear Nia from the night before: *We’re not spiraling.*

So instead of feeding the panic, he kept walking toward office hours with his notebook tucked under one arm and three targeted questions already written down. If the week was going to bring uncertainty, he wanted to meet it moving forward.

That was new.

A month ago he might have checked the score first, let it determine his mood, and carried that mood into the rest of the day like a weight. Now he was trying—sometimes awkwardly, but honestly—to live by the systems he had been building.

When he stepped into office hours, Dr. Brooks was seated at a table with a stack of papers, glasses low on his nose, and the unhurried expression of someone who had watched many students survive the same kind of October tension.

Malik sat down, opened his notebook, and said, “I’m trying not to confuse being anxious with being prepared.”

Dr. Brooks looked up and gave him a small, knowing smile. “That’s a very first-semester sentence.”

Malik laughed once. “Yeah. I figured.”

“Good,” Dr. Brooks said. “That means you’re paying attention to your own habits.”

They worked through Malik’s questions one by one. Not just answers—reasoning. Dr. Brooks kept returning to the same principle he had repeated often enough that it had begun living in Malik’s head even outside class.

“Show your reasoning clearly,” he said, tapping one line of Malik’s work. “When your thinking is visible, you can improve it. When it stays hidden, you can only guess.”

That landed more deeply than usual.

Because Malik knew the statement was true beyond calculus.

All semester he had been learning that hidden thinking was dangerous. Hidden confusion. Hidden pride. Hidden fear. Hidden assumptions about what being “good at math” was supposed to look like. FAMU’s mathematics culture kept asking him, gently but firmly, to bring his thinking into the light.

Not to embarrass him.
To sharpen him.

By the time he left office hours, his shoulders felt lighter.

Not because the week had become easy.
Because he had remembered he was not facing it alone.

Across campus, Nia was doing something that also looked small from the outside and mattered a lot from the inside.

She was helping another student after class untangle a difficult concept that had been haunting him since the exam week.

He kept apologizing before every question.

“Sorry, I know this is probably obvious.”

“Sorry, I should’ve gotten this already.”

“Sorry, I’m asking in a weird way.”

By the third apology, Nia set her pen down and looked at him with kind seriousness.

“You do not have to apologize for learning out loud,” she said.

He blinked.

She continued, calmer now, not scolding him but inviting him into a different posture. “That’s part of what we do here. You ask, we work it through, and if it still doesn’t make sense, we try another angle.”

Then she turned the page sideways and started sketching the concept visually.

That, too, had become one of her signatures.

When equations felt too rigid, she looked for shape.
When a procedure felt cold, she searched for the idea underneath it.
When somebody else began shutting down from embarrassment, she found a way to reopen the problem without reopening the wound.

The student’s face changed halfway through the explanation.
It was subtle, but she saw it.
The moment confusion stopped feeling permanent.

“Oh,” he said quietly. “So it’s not that I didn’t know anything. I just kept starting in the wrong place.”

Nia smiled. “Exactly. That happens to everybody.”

And at FAMU, she was learning, that kind of peer culture mattered. The Mathematics Department was rigorous, yes, but it was also full of people who took growth seriously. Students helped each other at whiteboards. Professors gave practical feedback instead of performing distance. Office hours felt like places where thinking could be repaired. Even the hard weeks were full of reminders that excellence here was not solitary.

Saturday brought game-day energy against North Carolina Central, and campus changed its rhythm without losing its academic pulse.

The air itself seemed brighter.
Music drifted across walkways.
Clusters of students moved with more color, more laughter, more anticipation. Green and orange stood out everywhere. The kind of school spirit that could make even a tired week feel a little more alive settled over the campus.

Malik appreciated the energy, but he was also determined not to let the day slide away from him. He spent part of the morning updating his internship and career checklist at a table near the student center, earbuds in, posture straight, forcing himself to remember that long-term goals deserved attention even during emotionally noisy weeks.

Nia, meanwhile, attended a student organization planning meeting that carried exactly the kind of tension she had been learning to manage: good opportunities competing for limited energy.

She contributed, took notes, offered a practical idea for an upcoming event, and then—this was the important part—left when she said she would.

No lingering yeses.
No volunteering herself into exhaustion.
No mistaking constant availability for leadership.

When she found Malik later near the game-day crowd, he noticed the difference immediately.

“You look less stretched,” he said.

She laughed. “That might be the nicest thing anybody’s said to me all week.”

“I’m serious.”

“I know.” She adjusted the strap on her bag. “I’m practicing selective excellence.”

He tilted his head. “Selective excellence?”

“Doing important things well instead of doing every possible thing half-tired.”

Malik nodded slowly. “That sounds like something a professor says five years before students understand it.”

“Then I’m ahead of schedule.”

They walked with the crowd for a while, letting the game-day atmosphere lift some of the strain out of the week. That was one of the underrated gifts of campus life. At FAMU, seriousness and joy did not always cancel each other out. A student could be worried about a quiz, proud of a professor’s praise, tired from studying, and still get caught smiling at the sheer communal force of a football Saturday.

That balance mattered.
It kept pressure from becoming the whole story.

By Sunday, the week turned inward again.

Homework was due. The reset had to happen. Malik carved out a two-hour deep-work block in the library and kept his promise to himself: no fake productivity, no rushing just to feel busy. He worked slowly enough to understand and steadily enough to finish.

The library carried its usual Sunday atmosphere—a kind of respectful determination. Pages turning. Laptop keys tapping. Low voices near the far tables. Students recovering from the week behind them while preparing for the week ahead.

Malik liked that about FAMU too. The campus knew how to gather around effort.

A little later, Nia led another whiteboard study circle, and this one felt especially alive.

Not loud.
Alive.

Students came in carrying different kinds of fatigue, but the board kept filling. Someone asked a question that unlocked another student’s confusion. A first-year student hesitated, then tried a solution in front of everybody. A sophomore volunteer reframed a mistake as a useful checkpoint instead of a failure.

Nia moved among them with markers in both hands, asking the questions she had come to trust.

“What does this represent?”
“What’s your first move?”
“What are we assuming here?”
“Can you explain it without the formula first?”

By the end of the session, even the tired students looked more anchored.

That night, she rewrote her plans for the coming days and circled three priorities. Again. Not because she had failed the first time, but because she was learning that discipline was not a one-time decision. It was maintenance. Recommitment. Choosing clarity over drift, then choosing it again when necessary.

Monday opened with a fresh calculus problem set and the familiar sense that October never allowed anybody to coast for long.

Malik practiced timed calculus drills that afternoon and noticed something he would not have expected a few weeks earlier: the timer still raised his pulse, but it no longer scrambled his identity. It pressured him, yes. It did not define him.

That distinction felt important.

Nearby, Nia was turning class notes into a concept map so detailed it looked almost architectural. Arrows moved across the page. Ideas linked to methods. Methods linked to interpretations. The whole thing looked less like study notes and more like a model for how learning could become visible.

When Malik saw it later, he shook his head.

“You really do think in maps.”

“And you really do think in checklists,” she replied.

“Fair.”

“Honestly,” she said, glancing at the page, “I think that’s why we work. You help structure pressure. I help expand it before it closes too fast.”

He thought about that for a second.

“That might be the nicest academically accurate thing anybody’s said to me.”

She grinned. “See? We’re both growing.”

Tuesday brought another round of office hours for Malik and another moment of peer mentorship for Nia. The structure of the week was simple on paper, but emotionally it carried a lot more.

People were waiting for grades.
People were replaying questions in their minds.
People were trying not to let uncertainty write a story larger than the facts.

Dr. Price, during a brief conversation that afternoon, said something Malik wrote down immediately.

“State assumptions before modeling,” the professor reminded him, then added, “That includes assumptions about yourself.”

Malik looked up. “You mean academically?”

“I mean generally,” Dr. Price said. “Students decide too quickly what one hard week means about who they are. That’s bad mathematics and bad self-judgment.”

Malik carried that line around for the rest of the day.

Bad mathematics and bad self-judgment.

It felt like one of those phrases that might stay with him for years.

Wednesday arrived with the concept-check tension still hovering, and this time Nia felt her own confidence wobble for a brief, honest stretch.

Not collapse.
Wobble.

She heard two students comparing scores from another class and, for a moment, the old comparison spiral tried to pull her in. Maybe she was behind. Maybe other people were steadier. Maybe everyone else had figured out how to do all of this without feeling stretched thin.

Then she stopped herself.

That old reflex had not told her the truth yet, and it was not going to start now.

She opened her notebook and wrote a private benchmark list instead:
- Did I prepare honestly?
