The questions slowed him down in the best way.

Across campus, Nia stood at a whiteboard leading a study circle that felt more confident than it had even a few weeks earlier. The students around her no longer gathered with the slightly desperate energy of people hoping someone smarter would rescue them. They came ready to think. Ready to explain. Ready to test ideas in public.

That change had not happened by accident.

Nia had built a culture in the room, and the culture reflected what she herself had been learning. She asked students to speak their reasoning. She let pauses happen. She made confusion discussable instead of embarrassing. She pushed for conceptual understanding before symbolic speed. In a department like FAMU Mathematics, where rigor and mentorship could live side by side, that kind of student leadership mattered.

At one point, a first-year student frowned at the board and said, “I keep wanting to jump to the formula, but I can tell that’s not the right move.”

Nia smiled. “That’s growth. The fact that you can hear the shortcut calling your name and still resist it—that’s growth.”

The group laughed, but the student nodded seriously.

“Okay,” Nia continued, drawing a clean line beneath the setup. “Then let’s earn the formula instead of hiding behind it.”

That sentence stayed with the room.

Later that evening, when the sounds of game-day excitement floated through campus in waves, Malik and Nia crossed paths near the mathematics building. The light was beginning to soften, and students drifted past them carrying backpacks, snacks, team gear, and the layered expressions of a spring semester in motion.

“How was the library?” Nia asked.

Malik exhaled. “Less dramatic than it used to be.”

“That is very high praise from you.”

“I think Dr. Price is rewiring my brain.”

She laughed. “Because of assumptions?”

“Yeah. I keep seeing how many mistakes come from solving a problem I haven’t actually defined.”

Nia nodded. “That applies to more than math.”

He looked at her. “You mean life?”

“I mean leadership,” she said. “And responsibility. And saying yes to the right things.”

That was the first quiet resolution of the week: the tension from Week Thirty-Two did not vanish in a dramatic speech. It began to resolve in practice. The answer to who they needed to become was not abstract. They needed to become more deliberate people.

Saturday, March 14 carried another full sports slate—baseball against Southern University, softball against Mississippi Valley State—and yet both of them protected academic time with noticeable maturity.

Malik worked through calculus drills on a timer, but his relationship to the timer had changed. He was no longer treating the clock like an opponent. He treated it like a training condition. When he made an error, he stopped, marked it, and named the reason for it instead of just erasing it and pretending it had not happened.

Rushed setup.
Skipped unit check.
Assumed pattern too early.

Each note turned a mistake into information.

Meanwhile, Nia rewrote her notes into a sprawling concept map that stretched across more pages than she had intended. Arrows connected ideas from class to prior units; small margin comments translated formal procedure into memorable language; starred sections marked ideas she knew she could teach someone else if necessary.

Dr. Brooks’s guidance—**show your reasoning clearly**—echoed in her mind as she reorganized the material. She realized that clear reasoning was not just something performed for professors on quizzes and exams. It was an act of respect toward one’s own thinking. When she clarified a concept fully, she trusted herself more.

Sunday, March 15 brought homework due, a weekly planning reset, and one more baseball game against Southern University. It also delivered one of the week’s warmest scenes.

Malik attended office hours carrying targeted questions, not because he was in collapse, but because he had learned that strong students did not wait until confusion became a crisis. Dr. Delgado reviewed his work carefully and then tapped a section near the top of the page.

“Your finish is fine,” she said. “But the setup is where your confidence really lives.”

Malik leaned closer.

“You reached the correct result,” she continued, “but I want you to notice something. The moment your setup became clean, the rest of the problem almost solved itself.”

He looked at the page and saw it immediately.

Dr. Delgado gave him a small nod. “Clean setup before solving. Not because neatness is decorative. Because clear beginnings protect thinking.”

That sentence landed deeply. Malik had always respected precision, but now he was understanding its emotional power too. A clean start could steady a mind.

Across campus, Nia was helping a peer who had become tangled in a difficult concept and, more painfully, embarrassed by that struggle.

“I should know this by now,” the student muttered.

Nia shook her head gently. “That sentence is usually where learning gets stuck.”

The student looked up.

“You don’t need shame,” Nia said. “You need a clearer picture.”

She spent the next stretch of time breaking the problem into visual pieces, asking questions instead of overwhelming the student with explanation. By the end, the student was not magically transformed into a master of the topic, but something more important had happened: the fear had loosened.

That mattered.

In the FAMU Mathematics Department, mentorship was not always formal. Sometimes it looked like a professor redirecting a thought in office hours. Sometimes it looked like a study-circle leader protecting the dignity of someone who needed another pass through the material. Sometimes it looked like peers refusing to let each other fail in silence.

Monday, March 16 shifted the week toward future-facing momentum. A new calculus problem set was assigned. Malik updated his internship and career checklist. Nia attended a student organization planning meeting.

This could have been a quieter day. Instead, it became the hinge of the chapter.

While reviewing options and notes he had been collecting over previous weeks, Malik realized that his checklist no longer felt like fantasy inventory. Research opportunities, internships, faculty relationships, summer plans—these were not random items on a productivity page. They were connected to the exact habits he had been building all semester. Show your reasoning. State assumptions. Ask better questions. Build consistency. Seek help early. Interpret evidence well.

It struck him with unusual force that readiness for future opportunity was already being rehearsed in his present discipline.

Nia had a parallel realization during the student organization meeting. Plans moved quickly. Excitement was real. Several students wanted to build more programming, more outreach, more visibility. Earlier in the year, she might have felt the old pull to take on too much simply because she cared. But now she listened with intention. She contributed meaningfully. She volunteered where her gifts actually strengthened the group.

Walking out of the meeting, she felt something she had not always associated with restraint.

Pride.

Not the brittle pride of being seen. The grounded pride of knowing she had acted wisely.

Tuesday, March 17 brought another library deep-work block for Malik, another whiteboard study circle for Nia, and baseball against Mercer University. The campus energy was again unmistakable, yet both of them seemed steadier inside it.

The study circle that afternoon became one of Nia’s best yet. Students arrived with more pointed questions than before, and instead of dissolving into side chatter or passive waiting, the group moved with purpose. She structured the session into short rounds: understand the problem, articulate the concept, solve carefully, compare reasoning.

At one point Malik arrived after his own work and stood near the back long enough to notice something new.

The room trusted her.

Not just because she was smart. Because she helped people think better.

Afterward, when most of the group had dispersed, he said it plainly.

“You know that’s leadership, right?”

Nia capped the marker and raised an eyebrow. “What, writing too much on whiteboards?”

“No,” he said. “Building a room where people stop hiding what they don’t understand.”

Her joking expression softened.

“That means a lot,” she said quietly.

He hesitated, then added, “I think this is what last week was pointing toward. Not just future opportunities. Practice becoming the kind of people who can carry them.”

She looked back at the board, now full of equations, arrows, circles, and corrected lines. “Yeah,” she said. “I think so too.”

Wednesday, March 18 brought the quiz or concept-check window, and the whole week narrowed to a point. This was one of those days when effort needed to become execution.

Malik returned to timed calculus drills before the assessment, but he did not chase speed recklessly. He used the timer as preparation, not intimidation. Nia revised her concept map again, tightening it until she could move through the material with confidence and flexibility. Dr. Price’s reminder about assumptions hovered in Malik’s mind; Dr. Brooks’s insistence on clear reasoning stayed with Nia.

They both felt the pressure.

But pressure no longer automatically translated into panic.

That was the real achievement.

After the quiz window, they met outside with the tired half-laughter of students who had just spent too much mental energy in too short a period of time.

“Well?” Nia asked.

“I survived without arguing with the page,” Malik said.

“That’s enormous growth.”

“And you?”

She grinned. “I think my concept map won at least two points for me.”

He laughed, then grew thoughtful. “You know what I’m realizing?”

“What?”

“I used to think feeling ready would mean not feeling tension.”

Nia nodded slowly. “But now?”

“Now I think readiness might mean being able to work clearly while the tension is still there.”

That was the second major resolution of the chapter, and perhaps the most important one. The question from the previous week—who would they need to become?—had found a deeper answer. They did not need to become fearless. They needed to become steady.

Thursday, March 19 closed the week without the spectacle of a major sporting event, but the emotional momentum was stronger than ever. Malik returned to office hours for targeted questions. Nia helped another peer with a difficult concept. Dr. Brooks’s reminder to show reasoning clearly returned once more, this time feeling less like instruction and more like identity.

When they met near the end of the day, the campus had entered that familiar late-afternoon mood where sunlight stretched long across the walkways and the rush of the day softened just enough for reflection.

Malik held his notebook under one arm. Nia had a marker stain on the side of her hand.

“You still answering last week’s question?” she asked.

He smiled. “I think this week answered part of it for us.”

She folded her arms, listening.

“We need to become people who are deliberate under pressure,” he said. “People who can help others while keeping our own foundation strong. People who don’t just want opportunity—people who can carry it well.”

Nia let the words settle.

Then she nodded. “That feels true.”

For a moment they stood in silence, watching students pass by the mathematics building, hearing the ordinary sounds of a university that was doing what it did best: shaping futures through daily work, community, rigor, and care.

The week had resolved something meaningful. The excitement from Week Thirty-Two had not gone unanswered. It had found form. Malik and Nia were no longer merely imagining a larger future. They were practicing for it.

And yet the week ended with a new tension rising naturally behind that resolution.

Because once you begin acting ready, another question appears almost immediately.

Who is watching?

Not in a paranoid sense. In a consequential one.

Professors noticed consistency. Peers noticed leadership. Departments noticed reliability. Opportunities often began long before formal invitations, quietly taking shape in the minds of people who observed how students moved through ordinary weeks like this one.

As Malik and Nia started walking across campus, that realization lingered between them with equal parts excitement and weight.

If they were becoming more ready,
if the FAMU Mathematics Department was helping shape them into students who could think clearly, mentor generously, and lead with discipline,
then it was only a matter of time before someone entrusted them with something bigger.

And when that moment came, it would not feel like a random surprise.

It would feel like the next line in a proof they had already begun to write.

---

# Week Thirty-Four: The Week Readiness Started Looking Real

The question that ended the previous week found its answer sooner than either Malik or Nia expected.

Who was watching?

By the beginning of Week Thirty-Four, the answer was not mysterious anymore.

Professors were watching. Classmates were watching. The students who drifted into study circles unsure of themselves were watching. The department was watching in that quiet academic way that did not always announce itself out loud, but noticed patterns with remarkable accuracy. At FAMU, effort could be seen. Consistency could be seen. The way a student carried themselves through an ordinary week—especially a crowded spring week—could reveal just as much as a test score.

That realization might have made earlier versions of Malik and Nia tense for the wrong reasons. Malik might have become overly controlled, trying to perform readiness instead of simply living it. Nia might have felt the old temptation to overextend herself, mistaking visibility for responsibility. But this week began differently.

Instead of panic, there was focus.

Instead of trying to look impressive, they both leaned more deeply into the habits that had already started changing them.

Friday, March 20, arrived with the layered hum of a real FAMU spring day. Baseball was on the road at Arkansas Pine Bluff. Softball traveled to Alabama State. The campus carried that familiar blend of motion and pride—students walking briskly across the grounds with backpacks and iced drinks, conversations about assignments mixing easily with updates about weekend games, tables outside student spaces catching pockets of laughter and debate. FAMU never felt like a place where academic life and student life had to compete for oxygen. They braided together. That was part of what made the rhythm of the university feel so full.

Malik spent part of the day updating his internship and career checklist, but unlike earlier in the semester, the document no longer felt like a nervous archive of future hopes. It looked more like a map that was beginning to connect. He had notes about deadlines, skills he wanted to sharpen, faculty he hoped to speak with, and possible summer directions. What struck him most was how practical it all felt now.

The future was no longer some giant dramatic doorway.

It was being built out of repeated Fridays like this one.

Later, in class, Dr. Delgado reminded students to keep their setups clean before rushing into solutions. “A messy beginning creates fake difficulty,” she said, writing a carefully organized first line on the board. “Sometimes the problem is not as confusing as your page makes it look.”

Malik almost smiled to himself. A few months earlier he would have received that as ordinary procedural advice. Now he heard the deeper lesson inside it. A clean setup was not just visual order. It was intellectual honesty. It meant not pretending to understand the structure before you actually did.

After class he stayed back for a moment, reviewing a half-worked example.

Dr. Delgado glanced at his notebook. “Better,” she said.

He looked up. “Better than before, or better than good?”

That made her laugh softly. “Better than rushed. Which is often the more important improvement.”

That sentence stayed with him for the rest of the day.

Across campus, Nia attended a student organization planning meeting that could easily have turned into one of those overloaded sessions where everyone had enthusiasm and nobody had boundaries. But Nia came in calmer than she once would have. She listened closely, took notes, and only volunteered where her energy could actually matter. She proposed one specific contribution rather than five vague ones, and because it was specific, people took it seriously.

One of the upperclassmen nodded and said, “That would really help. You always make things clearer when a plan gets too crowded.”

It was a small comment, but it landed.

Someone was watching.

Noticing.

And not for superficial reasons.

What they were noticing was judgment. Follow-through. The ability to bring order and clarity into busy spaces.

That evening Malik and Nia crossed paths near the mathematics building while the last gold light of the day stretched across the walkway.

“You look thoughtful,” Nia said.

“I’m trying to decide if that’s good news or pressure,” Malik replied.

“What is?”

“The fact that people are starting to notice things.”

Nia tilted her head. “I think it depends on whether you think you have to become someone new overnight.”

“And if I don’t?”

“Then it’s just confirmation,” she said. “Of what you’ve already been building.”

That steadied him more than she knew.

Saturday, March 21, brought another busy sports day—baseball again at Arkansas Pine Bluff and softball at Alabama State for a doubleheader—and still both of them protected the core of the day for academic growth.

Malik gave himself a two-hour deep-work block at the library. The room had its usual spring-semester atmosphere: laptops open, chargers stretched awkwardly toward outlets, whispered frustration, determined silence, the occasional relieved exhale when a solution finally clicked. There was something comforting about working among people who were all carrying serious goals in different forms.

He moved carefully through a difficult set of calculus work, pausing whenever he felt the urge to skip straight from intuition to execution. More and more, he could hear his professors’ voices as part of his own internal discipline.

State assumptions.
Clean setup.
Don’t let speed impersonate understanding.

At one point he caught himself trying to force a familiar pattern onto a problem that did not quite fit. The old version of him might have kept pushing anyway, hoping to wrestle the problem into submission. This time he stopped, crossed out the false start, and began again from the concept.

The reset cost him a few minutes.

It probably saved him twenty.

Elsewhere, Nia led a whiteboard study circle that felt increasingly like a real academic community rather than a loose gathering of anxious students. She opened the session by saying, “Today, I want us to picture the idea before we calculate it. If we can’t see what the math is doing, the symbols will start bossing us around.”

That line made the group laugh, but it also unlocked something.

They worked more slowly at first, sketching shapes, relationships, and conceptual connections before moving into algebra. A student who usually stayed quiet raised a hand and said, “I think I finally understand why this step happens instead of just memorizing that it happens.”

Nia smiled immediately. “That’s the whole point.”

In moments like that, the culture of the FAMU Mathematics Department shone clearly. Rigor was never treated as coldness. High standards did not require humiliation. Students were pushed to think well, but they were also given room to grow into that standard through mentorship, repetition, and community.

Sunday, March 22, brought homework due, a weekly planning reset, and one more baseball game at Arkansas Pine Bluff. It was the kind of day that could disappear into maintenance work if handled carelessly. Instead, it became one of the most quietly important days of the chapter.

Malik did timed calculus drills and then reviewed the mistakes without flinching from them. He had become disciplined about naming the source of each miss.

Unclear initial condition.
Visualized too late.
Moved before checking what was actually being asked.

The notes no longer felt like evidence against him. They were evidence for him—proof that he was learning how to diagnose himself accurately.

Nia spent part of the day rewriting class notes into a large concept map, drawing connections between procedures, interpretations, and recurring ideas. Dr. Bennett’s phrase—interpret numbers as evidence—stayed with her as she worked. She realized it applied to her own life too. The week itself was producing evidence. Her steadier schedule, sharper choices, stronger study sessions, and better boundaries were not small side notes. They were data. They showed that maturity was not theoretical anymore.

When they met later for a short planning check-in, the tone between them was easy.

“I think I’m finally respecting the reset day,” Malik said.

Nia nodded. “That’s because you stopped treating it like a day for catching up on guilt.”

He laughed. “That is painfully accurate.”

“It’s supposed to help the week feel possible,” she said. “Not punish you for the last one.”

Monday, March 23, moved the story forward with renewed academic pressure. A calculus problem set was assigned. Softball traveled to Auburn. Malik attended office hours with targeted questions, and Nia helped a peer with a difficult concept.

This was the meaningful resolution promised by the previous week’s anticipation: the sense that bigger things were coming did not explode into a dramatic announcement. It resolved at the start of this week through recognition that they were already operating inside bigger responsibility. Their ordinary choices now had influence.

During office hours, Malik asked a question that was narrower and sharper than the ones he used to bring.

Dr. Price looked over his page and said, “You’re asking better questions.”

Malik blinked. “Because I know more?”

“Partly,” Dr. Price said. “But mostly because you’re thinking before you ask. That matters.”

It was such a simple comment that it almost passed by unnoticed. But it stayed with him because it named the exact change he had been trying to build.

Meanwhile, Nia sat with a classmate who had become tangled in a concept and, worse, had started narrating the struggle as a personal failure.

Nia stopped that quickly.

“No,” she said gently. “You are not behind as a person. You are just unclear on one part of the chain.”

Then she broke the idea apart with patience, asking the student to explain each step back in their own words. By the end, the student sat up straighter.

“Okay,” they said, almost surprised. “That actually makes sense now.”

Nia felt a quiet satisfaction that had less to do with being helpful and more to do with what kind of mathematician she was becoming. Someone who could understand deeply enough to make others less afraid.

Tuesday, March 24, had no major sports event attached to it, but it still carried momentum. Malik updated his internship checklist again, this time adding action items rather than just possibilities. Nia attended another planning meeting and noticed she was increasingly being treated as someone dependable, not just enthusiastic.

That distinction mattered.

Dependable was heavier than impressive.

Dependable meant someone could build with you.

Wednesday, March 25, tightened the week with a quiz or concept-check window and baseball at home against Troy University. The home-game energy lifted campus noticeably. Students moved with extra buzz, and the atmosphere around FAMU felt proud and bright. But inside the math spaces, the pressure was real.

Malik returned to the library for another deep-work block, and Nia led another whiteboard study circle before the concept check. There was tension in both of them, but it was cleaner tension now—not chaotic, not self-defeating. It was the tension of people who cared and had prepared.

Before the quiz, Nia told the group, “Don’t let the first hard-looking problem decide your emotional state. Read. Breathe. Find structure.”

Malik, hearing that from nearby, thought: that’s exactly what she has learned to do herself.

The concept check came and went in the concentrated blur familiar to every student deep into spring semester. By the time it ended, both of them looked tired, but not shaken.

Outside, with the sounds of the baseball crowd beginning to rise from a distance, they exchanged the kind of relieved smile that only means something after honest effort.

“How’d it feel?” Nia asked.

“Fair,” Malik said. “Not easy. But fair.”

She nodded. “Same.”

Then he added, “I noticed something.”

“What?”

“I didn’t spend the whole time trying to prove I belonged.”

Nia’s expression softened. “That’s big.”

He looked out across campus. “I just worked the problems.”

Thursday, March 26, ended the week with no flashy event, which made it the perfect day for reflection. Malik practiced calculus drills with a timer once more. Nia rewrote her notes into an even cleaner concept map. Dr. Okafor’s familiar wisdom—visualize the concept first—echoed through both of their routines in different ways.

By late afternoon they found themselves walking slowly past the mathematics building again, the place that had quietly held so many of their turning points.

“This week felt different,” Nia said.

Malik nodded. “Yeah. Less like chasing readiness.”

“More like using it?”

He smiled. “Exactly.”

For a while they walked in silence, watching students move through the ordinary beauty of campus life—friends greeting each other across the yard, someone hurrying toward a meeting, someone else standing outside laughing with a professor after a serious conversation had clearly turned lighter. The life of the university felt big around them, but not impersonal. That was one of FAMU’s gifts. Students could grow here without disappearing.

At last Nia said, “You know what I think the real shift is?”

“What?”

“We’re not just responding to the semester anymore. We’re shaping it.”

That was true, and both of them knew it.

But the week did not end in complete rest.

It ended with a new and sharpened anticipation.

Because once readiness starts looking real—once professors notice, once peers trust you, once your habits begin producing visible steadiness—another possibility rises right behind it.

Responsibility expands.

Not someday. Soon.

As they headed in separate directions, each carrying notes, unfinished work, and the strange satisfaction of a demanding week handled well, the feeling that followed them was not fear.

It was something brighter and heavier at the same time.

Expectation.

They had spent weeks building discipline, clarity, and trust.

Now it felt increasingly likely that the FAMU Mathematics Department, and the larger world opening around it, would ask them to do something more with all three.

And for the first time, that possibility did not feel distant.

It felt close enough to hear approaching.

---

# Week Thirty-Five: When Expectation Turned Into Invitation

The expectation that had followed Malik and Nia at the end of the previous week did not arrive as pressure from nowhere.

It arrived as invitations.

By Friday morning, March 27, the FAMU Mathematics Department felt especially alive. Spring had reached that point in the semester when the days seemed brighter even when students were tired, and campus energy moved in several directions at once. Baseball was hosting Alabama State University. Softball was away at Bethune-Cookman. Conversations about games floated alongside talk of quizzes, office hours, deadlines, and plans for April. There was movement everywhere, but inside that motion Malik and Nia were beginning to notice something new.

People were not only noticing that they worked hard.

People were starting to lean toward them.

That was the real answer to the expectation they had sensed. Responsibility was not arriving as some dramatic burden dropped onto their shoulders. It was showing up through classmates asking a second question instead of stopping at the first. It was showing up through professors offering one more layer of explanation because they trusted the effort would be used well. It was showing up through peers treating them not just like fellow students, but like fellow builders of the culture around them.

That morning Malik went to office hours carrying a page of carefully narrowed questions. A few months earlier, he would have walked in with either too much pride or too much vague frustration. Now his notes were neat, specific, and honest. He had circled one recurring issue in red: where his modeling instincts were strong but his setup still sometimes got ahead of his assumptions.

Dr. Bennett reviewed the page, tapped the margin, and said, “This is better. You’re bringing evidence now, not panic.”

Malik laughed once under his breath. “That may be the best compliment I’ve gotten all semester.”

“It should be,” Dr. Bennett said. “Evidence gives us something to work with. Anxiety just fills the room.”

Then she leaned back and added, “Walk me through what these numbers are saying before you tell me what answer you want.”

That phrasing changed the whole conversation. Instead of defending a result, Malik explained the story of the problem. He named the assumptions. He identified where the structure bent. He pointed out where he had made an interpretive leap too early.

By the end of the session, Dr. Bennett nodded with visible approval.

“You’re thinking more like someone preparing for real professional judgment,” she said. “Keep that. The math matters, but the habit underneath it matters more.”

Those words followed him outside like a second heartbeat.

Professional judgment.

It sounded larger than one assignment. Larger than one class.

Across campus, Nia was helping a peer untangle a difficult concept before the day fully slipped into its busier rhythms. They sat at a table where notebooks, highlighters, and half-finished drinks had created the usual ecosystem of student survival. Her classmate kept apologizing for not “getting it fast enough,” and Nia finally held up a hand.

“Stop insulting yourself while you’re trying to learn,” she said, gentle but direct.

The student blinked. “I’m not—”

“You are,” Nia said. “And it’s wasting energy. Let’s use that energy on the concept instead.”

She pulled the problem apart slowly, sketching relationships before symbols, tracing the logic in a way that made the mathematics feel less like a wall and more like a path. When the student finally said, “Wait—so that’s why the step changes there,” Nia smiled in immediate recognition.

“Yes,” she said. “Now you’re seeing it.”

There was joy in that moment, but also something deeper. Nia had always loved understanding. What was growing in her now was a love of making understanding transferable. It was one thing to shine alone. It was another thing to help a room grow clearer.

By late afternoon, the athletic buzz around campus had grown louder. Students were talking about baseball. Phones lit up with updates from softball. The whole place carried that FAMU quality of collective life—serious, proud, social, and full of momentum. Malik and Nia crossed paths near the mathematics building just as the day softened into evening.

“You look like office hours went well,” Nia said.

“They did,” Malik replied. “Dr. Bennett basically told me I’m becoming less dramatic with my data.”

Nia laughed. “That sounds exactly like something you needed to hear.”

“It really does.” He paused. “How was your day?”

“A student I helped finally stopped panicking long enough to understand what they were looking at.”

“That’s a win.”

“It is,” she said. Then, after a beat: “I think last week’s feeling was right.”

“The expectation?”

She nodded. “Yeah. But it feels less like somebody’s judging us and more like… people are making room for us.”

Malik considered that. “An invitation.”

“Exactly.”

Saturday, March 28, widened that idea.

Baseball again faced Alabama State. Softball remained at Bethune-Cookman. Campus had the feel of a weekend that still belonged partly to school, partly to community, and partly to the future. Malik spent the morning updating his internship and career checklist, but this time it was no longer just a private exercise in ambition. He added names of faculty to follow up with. He refined deadlines. He listed skills he could now discuss with more confidence because they were no longer hypothetical.

At one point he stared at the page and realized something subtle but important: the checklist was beginning to describe a person who already existed in early form.

Not a future fantasy version of himself.

Him.

A little later, he ran into Dr. Price, who glanced at the folder in his hand and asked, “Planning ahead?”

“Trying to,” Malik said.

Dr. Price gave him a thoughtful look. “Good. But don’t just collect options. State your assumptions before you model your future the same way you do in class.”

Malik smiled immediately. “You really can turn anything into a lesson.”

“That’s because anything can become one,” Dr. Price said.

The advice landed harder than it might have months earlier. Malik understood now that assumptions did not only belong in equations. They belonged in career decisions, scheduling choices, and the stories students told themselves about who they were becoming.

While Malik refined his checklist, Nia joined a student organization planning meeting that could easily have turned noisy and unfocused. Instead, she helped steady it. She listened, waited, then began organizing ideas into clear next steps.

“What do we actually need by next week?” she asked.

The room quieted.

“What can wait?”

Someone started listing everything at once. Nia smiled, then reached for a marker and wrote two columns on the board: **Now** and **Later**.

“Let’s protect the work that matters most,” she said.

An upperclassman watched her do it and later told her, “You know, you have a way of lowering the chaos in a room without lowering the energy.”

Nia thanked her, but the sentence stayed with her long after the meeting ended. It named the leadership style she had been slowly, almost accidentally, growing into. Not loud leadership. Not performative leadership. Useful leadership.

Sunday, March 29, carried baseball against Alabama State and the usual homework-due, reset-the-week discipline that spring students either learned to respect or paid for later. Malik protected a two-hour deep-work block in the library. Nia led a whiteboard study circle. Both of them sensed the week tightening toward Monday already.

In the library Malik worked through problems with unusual patience. Whenever he felt the impulse to rush, he stopped and asked himself what the problem was actually asking him to see. The old instinct—to prove competence quickly—still flickered, but it no longer ran the room.

Across campus, Nia stood at the whiteboard with dry-erase marker on her fingers and a semicircle of students around her.

“Don’t chase the answer first,” she told them. “Tell me the shape of the idea.”

A student tried. Another corrected part of it. Someone else connected it back to an earlier concept. Bit by bit, the board filled with not just procedures, but reasoning. The session became exactly the kind of undergraduate math culture the department quietly fostered: collaborative, rigorous, generous, and proud of thinking well together.

Later that evening Malik and Nia met briefly to reset for the week ahead.

“I think the study circle is changing,” Nia said.

“How?”

“People are actually trusting each other enough to sound confused out loud.”

Malik nodded. “That might be the biggest upgrade possible.”

Monday, March 30, brought a new calculus problem set and a fresh wave of seriousness. Malik practiced timed calculus drills. Nia rewrote her notes into a concept map. The work itself was not glamorous, but it mattered. This was how strong weeks were built: not through one dramatic performance, but through clean, repeated decisions.

When they compared notes later, Malik admitted, “I’m getting faster, but only because I’ve stopped treating every timer like a threat.”

Nia gave him a knowing look. “So the timer isn’t the enemy anymore?”

“No,” he said. “Just a mirror.”

“That,” she said, “is extremely math-department growth of you.”

Tuesday, March 31, had no giant academic announcement attached to it, but the day still mattered. Softball was at Troy. Malik returned to office hours with targeted questions. Nia helped another peer with a difficult concept, and this time a second student sat down nearby to listen in. Then a third.

By the end, it had become an unofficial mini-session.

Nia looked around and laughed. “Okay, apparently this is a class now.”

One of the students smiled and said, “You explain things without making people feel dumb.”

That compliment hit her harder than the student probably knew. It reached something central in her—not just what kind of student she wanted to be, but what kind of scholar, mentor, and future teacher or researcher she might become.

Meanwhile, Dr. Okafor looked over Malik’s question during office hours and said, “Before we solve this, visualize it.”

Malik paused, then described the structure out loud.

“Good,” Dr. Okafor said. “Now solve the picture you just created.”

That sentence unlocked the rest. Malik left with more than a corrected approach. He left with a clearer sense that conceptual confidence was not reserved for some elite future version of himself. He was building it now.

Wednesday, April 1, sharpened everything with a quiz or concept-check window. Malik updated his internship checklist one more time before class, grounding himself by looking at the larger path beyond one day’s stress. Nia joined another organization meeting but kept her boundaries firm, refusing the old urge to overpromise just because she cared.

The quiz itself carried tension, of course. But it was cleaner tension than before. Productive tension. The kind born from preparation rather than panic.

Afterward, they found each other outside.

“How bad?” Nia asked.

“Not bad,” Malik said. “Not effortless either.”

She exhaled. “Same.”

Then she looked at him more closely. “You don’t seem rattled.”

“I’m not,” he said, sounding mildly surprised by his own answer. “I think I trust my process more now.”

Nia smiled. “That might be the best thing either of us could’ve said this semester.”

Thursday, April 2, closed the week with a full campus rhythm again: baseball traveling to Prairie View A&M, softball hosting Alabama A&M, and both Malik and Nia returning to the patterns that had changed them. Malik held another library deep-work block. Nia led another whiteboard study circle. The week’s scenes echoed each other on purpose, but they did not feel repetitive. They felt cumulative.

That afternoon, after her study circle ended, one of the younger students lingering near the board asked Nia, “Are y’all doing this again next week?”

Before she could answer, Malik—who had just arrived from the library—said, “Looks like you have regulars now.”

Nia looked at the board, at the students waiting, at the easy expectation in their faces.

And suddenly the real resolution of the week became clear.

Expectation had turned into invitation.

Invitation had turned into trust.

And trust was beginning to turn into leadership.

“Yeah,” Nia said at last, smiling. “We’re doing it again next week.”

They walked out together into the bright, busy late afternoon of FAMU, where students moved between games, classes, meetings, and futures still under construction. The campus felt full in the best way—alive with evidence that learning here was not only about grades, but about becoming the kind of person other people could build with.

For a while neither of them said anything.

Then Malik broke the silence.

“I think this is the part nobody really explains to you when you first get here.”

“What part?” Nia asked.

“That if you keep showing up the right way, one day people stop just helping you grow.” He glanced back toward the building. “They start expecting you to help hold the door open for somebody else.”

Nia’s expression softened into something proud and thoughtful all at once. “That sounds exactly like FAMU.”

“It does.”

The week could have ended there, calm and complete.

But spring never stayed still for long.

Because once students begin to be trusted, once the department starts quietly making room for them, the next challenge is never far behind. Leadership brings visibility. Visibility brings new choices. And choices, especially in April, have a way of arriving faster than expected.

As the light shifted over campus and the sounds of games, conversations, and hurried footsteps braided into one familiar FAMU song, Malik and Nia kept walking with the steady fatigue of people who had earned their week.

Behind them, the whiteboard still held traces of the day’s thinking.

Ahead of them, April was opening.

And it already felt like it was bringing something that would ask more of both of them than simply doing well on their own.

---

# Week Thirty-Six: The Week the Door Opened Wider

April did not begin by asking Malik and Nia whether they were ready for more.

It simply arrived with more already in motion.

The unresolved feeling from the end of the previous week—the sense that trust was turning into leadership and that leadership would soon demand something larger—found its answer early on Friday, April 3. The answer was not a single dramatic crisis. It was a week shaped by overlapping pressures: athletic energy across campus, repeated exam-window tension, office hours, study circles, concept checks, and the quiet realization that other students were no longer only watching Malik and Nia grow.

They were beginning to depend on them.

That Friday morning FAMU felt bright, busy, and alive in the particular way it often did when academics and athletics were both pulling at the edges of the day. Baseball was at Prairie View A&M. Softball was hosting Alabama A&M. Students moved across campus in clusters, carrying backpacks, water bottles, game talk, deadlines, and the slightly sharpened expressions that appeared whenever a demanding stretch of the semester came into view.

Malik had claimed a table early and set a timer on his phone before opening his calculus review packet.

A few months ago, the timer would have felt like an accusation.

Now it felt like training.

He worked through one derivative problem, then another, checking not only speed but whether he was staying clean in the setup. Whenever his pencil moved too fast, he heard the accumulated voices of the semester correcting him: Dr. Brooks telling him to show his reasoning clearly, Dr. Delgado insisting on a clean setup before solving, Dr. Price asking him to state his assumptions before he started treating an answer like destiny.

Across from him, Nia spread out her notes and began transforming pages of lecture material into a concept map. She used arrows, circles, color, and careful spacing, building relationships instead of just lists. To anyone passing by, it might have looked decorative. It was not. It was one of the ways she had learned to keep herself from drowning in information by forcing ideas to reveal their structure.

One first-year student slowed near their table.

“Y’all already studying for next week?” he asked.

Malik glanced up. “Not next week,” he said. “This week.”

The student blinked. “That bad?”

Nia smiled. “Not bad. Just real.”

The student laughed, but then lingered. “Can I sit for a minute? I’m trying to figure out where to even start.”

And just like that, the week’s answer began taking shape.

The invitation from the previous week had become something more practical. Malik slid his review packet slightly to the side. Nia turned her notebook so the student could see the map. What followed was not a full tutoring session, not yet, but a small beginning: a conversation about how to organize study time before the pressure became panic.

“Don’t start with everything,” Malik said. “Start with what keeps showing up.”

“And connect topics before you memorize steps,” Nia added. “If the ideas are isolated in your head, the test will feel harder than it is.”

By the time the student left, he looked lighter.

“So,” Nia said once he was gone, “I think your comment from yesterday came true fast.”

Malik capped his pen. “About holding the door open?”

She nodded.

He looked around the student union, the courtyard beyond, the movement of campus. “Yeah,” he said quietly. “I think the door just got wider.”

Saturday, April 4, kept baseball at Prairie View A&M on people’s minds, but academically it gave Malik and Nia a different kind of momentum. Malik went to office hours with targeted questions, his page arranged with uncommon discipline: not a scattered list of complaints, but specific points where his reasoning broke down under time pressure. Dr. Delgado reviewed the page in silence first, then nodded.

“This,” she said, tapping the margin, “is the work of someone who is learning how to diagnose instead of just react.”

Malik exhaled a small laugh. “I’m trying.”

“I can see that.” She pointed to one problem. “Your algebra is not the main issue here. Your setup is too eager. You are solving before the problem has fully introduced itself.”

That sentence landed with the force of something true far beyond mathematics.

Dr. Delgado continued, “Clean setup before solving. Not because neatness is pretty. Because clarity protects you from avoidable mistakes.”

Malik rewrote the first lines of the problem more slowly.

“There,” she said. “Now continue.”

This time the rest unfolded with less friction.

Meanwhile Nia spent part of the afternoon helping a classmate who had grown quiet in recent weeks. At first he insisted he was “mostly fine,” which in college often meant exactly the opposite. Nia did not push too hard. She simply asked him to explain the concept in his own words. Halfway through the explanation, he stopped and rubbed his forehead.

“I know pieces of it,” he admitted. “But it doesn’t stay connected.”

Nia nodded with immediate recognition. “That’s actually useful information.”

He looked surprised. “Useful?”

“Yes. Because now we know the issue isn’t that you’re incapable. It’s that the ideas are floating instead of attaching.”

She drew the concept visually, building the structure slowly enough for him to see it emerge. When the understanding clicked, his posture changed.

“Oh,” he said, almost laughing. “That actually makes sense now.”

“That’s because it always could,” Nia replied. “You just needed a better route into it.”

Moments like that energized her, but this semester had taught her to watch the difference between meaningful service and overextension. So after helping him, she closed her notebook instead of opening it for three more hours of unscheduled work. Growth, for Nia, now included stopping before generosity turned into exhaustion.

Sunday, April 5, brought homework due and the usual weekly planning reset. The campus pace softened slightly, but not enough for anyone serious to mistake it for rest. Malik updated his internship and career checklist in the morning, making notes not only about deadlines and applications but about skills he wanted to discuss more confidently: quantitative reasoning, consistency under pressure, collaborative problem-solving, and communication. He was beginning to understand that the strongest version of his résumé would not be a list of claims but a record of habits already forming.

Nia attended a student organization planning meeting that could easily have expanded into a chaotic swirl of good intentions. Instead, she helped narrow it.

“What absolutely needs to happen this week?” she asked, marker in hand.

The room went quiet.

“What would be nice but not necessary?”

An upperclassman grinned. “You really do know how to save a meeting.”

Nia laughed, but she still drew two columns on the board: **Must Move** and **Can Wait**.

It was a small act, yet it echoed the same lesson she was learning academically. Clear structure was not restrictive. It was what allowed energy to become useful.

Later that evening Malik and Nia met outside with their planners, notes, and the sky slowly turning gold over campus.

“This week feels crowded,” Malik said.

“It is crowded,” Nia answered. “But I don’t think crowded has to mean chaotic.”

He looked at her, half-smiling. “That sounds like something you should put on a poster for the department.”

“Only if you put ‘clean setup before solving’ under it.”

They laughed, but beneath the joke was a real sense of readiness. The coming exam windows were no longer abstract. They were approaching fast.

Monday, April 6, sharpened the week. A new calculus problem set was assigned. Malik protected a two-hour deep-work block in the library, stacking his materials carefully and refusing to let his attention scatter. Across the table from him sat two students from an earlier study circle, and when one of them got stuck, he hesitated before asking.

“Can I interrupt for one question?”

Malik looked up. Months earlier he might have guarded his focus too tightly, afraid that helping someone else would cost him momentum. Now he understood something more mature: sometimes explaining a concept clarified your own thinking.

“Yeah,” he said. “Show me where it goes sideways.”

The student pointed to the line where the setup had drifted.

Malik smiled despite himself. “Okay. First thing? You started solving before the problem fully introduced itself.”

The phrase was so exactly Dr. Delgado’s that both students laughed.

“Wow,” the other one said. “You sound like a professor.”

Malik shook his head. “Not even close. But the advice is good.”

Elsewhere, Nia led a whiteboard study circle that afternoon, and what began as a handful of students soon became a small crowd. She wrote the week’s major ideas at the top of the board and drew lines between them before anyone reached for shortcuts.

“Visualize the concept first,” she said, echoing Dr. Okafor. “Then let the symbols tell the same story.”

A quiet student in the back raised a hand and asked a question so tentative it nearly dissolved in the room. Nia took it seriously immediately. She repeated it clearly, answered it without hurry, and then asked if anyone else had wondered the same thing.

Three more hands went up.

That moment changed the tone of the room. Confusion stopped feeling embarrassing and started feeling communal. The study circle became exactly what the FAMU Mathematics Department at its best encouraged: rigorous, welcoming, confident without being cruel, and deeply invested in helping undergraduates grow together.

Tuesday, April 7, marked the first midterm-style exam window of the week.

Campus felt different from the start of the morning. Even the students joking on the walkways seemed to do it with a little extra edge. Malik ran timed calculus drills one last time before class, but instead of fighting the clock, he treated each problem like a conversation between method and judgment. Nia rewrote her notes into a fresh concept map, not because she had forgotten the material but because redrawing the structure helped steady her mind.

When they met briefly before the exam, neither tried to pretend complete calm.

“You good?” Malik asked.

“I’m focused,” Nia said. “Which is not the same thing as relaxed.”

“That’s honest.”

“You?”

“Nervous,” he admitted. “But not scattered.”

She nodded approvingly. “That’s growth.”

The exam itself demanded exactly what the semester had been trying to teach them: reasoning under pressure, clean setup, conceptual clarity, discipline. There were moments when Malik felt his pulse rise, but instead of rushing, he wrote one line at a time and forced himself to state what he knew before jumping to what he hoped. Nia encountered one problem that at first seemed familiar and then subtly shifted shape. For a second she felt the old urge to overwork every possibility. Instead she paused, sketched the idea, and let the structure reveal the path.

When they came out, the relief was real but incomplete.

“That was fair,” Malik said.

“Fair and sneaky,” Nia replied.

He laughed. “Yes. Exactly that.”

But there was pride there too. Not pride in perfection—something more durable than that. Pride in who they had become under academic stress.

Wednesday, April 8, raised the stakes again with both a quiz or concept-check window and another midterm-style pressure point, while baseball headed to Troy University. Malik returned to office hours for targeted questions about what still felt unstable after Tuesday. Nia helped a peer with a difficult concept, and by now the pattern had become unmistakable: students sought them out not only because they were strong, but because they were trustworthy.

Dr. Brooks listened to Malik explain a problem and then said, “You’re stronger when you let your reasoning be visible. Don’t hide the path trying to look fast.”

That advice met him exactly where he needed it.

At nearly the same hour, Nia sat with two students outside the math building, walking them through an idea one careful layer at a time. One of them finally said, “I thought asking this meant I was behind.”

Nia shook her head. “No. Asking before the confusion hardens is one of the smartest things you can do.”

By Thursday, April 9, the final exam-window tension of the week had settled over campus like weather. Malik updated his career checklist again, but this time he added a note at the top: **Keep building the habits that make future opportunities real.** Nia attended another student organization meeting, more measured now, saying yes where it mattered and no where it protected her academic priorities.

That evening they met after the last major pressure point of the week, both carrying the tiredness that only honest effort creates.

For a while they sat without talking, watching students cross campus in the blue-gold light of early evening.

Then Malik said, “I think I finally understand something.”

Nia turned toward him. “What?”

“That being part of this department isn’t just about surviving the hard classes.” He looked back toward the building. “It’s about learning how to help create the kind of environment where other people can survive them too.”

Nia’s expression softened. “Yeah,” she said. “I think that’s what this week was really about.”

He glanced at her. “And I think it’s only getting bigger from here.”

That was the week’s final truth.

The anticipation from Week Thirty-Five had been resolved: yes, trust had turned into responsibility. Yes, the door had opened wider. Yes, Malik and Nia were becoming the kind of students others could lean on.

But the resolution did not close the story.

It opened the next one.

Because as they sat there, exhausted but steadier than they had been at the start of the year, both of them could feel another layer of spring approaching. More deadlines. Bigger decisions. More visible opportunities. More chances to lead, to choose wisely, to protect their own growth while making room for someone else’s.

The week had proven they could carry more.

Now April seemed ready to test exactly how much more that would be.

Around them, FAMU still moved with its familiar mix of excellence, warmth, pressure, and possibility. Students headed toward residence halls, meetings, games, dining spots, and late-night study plans. Somewhere inside the mathematics building, whiteboards still held traces of proofs, diagrams, assumptions, and half-erased breakthroughs.

Malik stood first and slung his bag over one shoulder.

“Same time next week?” he asked.

Nia smiled, tired but bright. “For studying or saving the department one conversation at a time?”

He grinned. “Apparently both.”

They started walking.

Behind them was a week that had asked for focus, steadiness, and care—and had received all three.

Ahead of them was a stretch of spring that no longer felt like something happening to them.

It felt like something they were growing ready to shape.

And that made the next week feel not just busy, but electric.

---

# Week Thirty-Seven: The Weight They Could Carry

The promise at the end of the previous week did not stay abstract for long.

By the time Thursday, April 10 arrived, the question was no longer whether Malik and Nia were becoming the kind of students other people leaned on. That question had already been answered. The new question was harder, sharper, and far more revealing:

Could they carry that trust without losing themselves?

Spring at FAMU had a way of making every day feel full before the day had even properly begun. Athletics gave the campus one layer of energy, academics gave it another, and somewhere between those forces students learned whether they would be pushed around by the semester or learn to move with purpose inside it. That week, baseball was on the road at Jackson State, softball was battling Alabama State, and the mathematics department kept moving with the quiet certainty of people who understood that April did not reward drifting.

Thursday morning opened warm and bright, with the kind of sunlight that made the red brick and green spaces around campus seem almost too vivid to be ordinary. Students crossed paths carrying laptops, folders, drinks, deadlines, and fragments of conversations about quizzes, travel schedules, group chats, and weekend plans. The whole campus felt awake.

Malik reached the library early for a two-hour deep-work block. He had learned by then that deep work did not happen by accident. It had to be claimed. Protected. Built on purpose.

He spread out his notebook, his problem set, and the sheet where he had begun keeping what he privately called his recovery rules: slow the first line down, state assumptions, clean setup before solving, do not confuse panic with urgency.

He had barely gotten through the first problem when someone approached his table.

“Hey,” the student said carefully, “are you busy-busy or just studying busy?”

Malik looked up and almost smiled.

There was a difference.

“Depends,” he said. “What happened?”

The student held up a page. “I think I’m mixing up the idea and the procedure again.”

Malik gestured toward the empty chair beside him. “Sit down for five minutes.”

The student sat immediately, relief written all over his face.

Malik scanned the page. The work was not careless. That was the first thing he noticed. It was anxious. The lines moved too quickly, like someone trying to outrun confusion.

He tapped the setup. “You’re not starting in the wrong place because you can’t do this,” he said. “You’re starting in the wrong place because you’re trying to get to the answer before the problem tells you what it is.”

The student stared at him. “That is exactly what it feels like.”

Malik nodded. “Yeah. I know.”

For the next few minutes he walked him through the structure instead of the shortcut. He showed him how to slow the opening, identify what was known, and force the page to become evidence rather than a guessing contest. It was the same discipline FAMU professors had been pressing into him all year, and now it passed through him with surprising naturalness.

When the student left, he said, “Thanks, man. You explain it like it’s fixable.”

Malik sat with that sentence for a while after the student was gone.

Like it’s fixable.

There was something powerful in that. Not just mathematically, but humanly. That was part of what the FAMU Mathematics Department did when it was operating at its best: it treated difficulty as something to work through, not proof that someone did not belong.

Later that afternoon Nia led a whiteboard study circle, and the room filled faster than usual. She noticed it right away. More first-years than normal. A few students from classes adjacent to hers. Even one student who usually stayed near the door as if ready to leave at the first sign of embarrassment.

Nia uncapped the marker and wrote the week’s big idea across the board in large, clear letters.

“Before we solve anything,” she said, “we’re going to make the concept visible.”

A student near the front laughed lightly. “You always say that.”

“Because it keeps being true,” Nia replied.

A few people smiled, and the room relaxed.

She began drawing the relationships between the ideas rather than listing steps. Curves, arrows, labels, connections. The board turned into a map of thought. As she talked, she kept asking questions instead of delivering a performance.

“What changes here?”

“What stays the same?”

“What would this look like if you had to explain it to someone who missed class?”

The student by the door finally raised his hand.

“I get lost,” he admitted, “when it stops looking like the homework and starts looking unfamiliar.”

Nia nodded immediately. “Good. That’s an important problem.”

He blinked. “Good?”

“Yes,” she said. “Because now we’re naming the actual issue. Not ‘I’m bad at math.’ The issue is transfer. That’s workable.”

Something changed in the room after that. Several students straightened. Someone else admitted they had the same problem. Another said timed quizzes made the whole page feel unfamiliar even when the concept wasn’t. What might once have become a quiet spiral of individual shame turned into a shared academic conversation.

That was leadership too, Nia realized. Not just having answers, but creating a space where confusion could be named without humiliation.

Friday, April 11, carried sports energy across campus again as baseball remained at Jackson State and softball stayed in action against Alabama State. The atmosphere around FAMU held that familiar spring contradiction: festive at the edges, demanding at the center.

Malik spent part of the day doing timed calculus drills. The timer still elevated his pulse, but no longer in the same destructive way. He had learned how to keep speed from becoming recklessness. Each time he finished a problem, he checked not only whether the answer was right but whether the process would survive pressure.

Nia, meanwhile, rewrote her notes into a concept map so detailed it looked almost architectural. She had discovered that rewriting notes this way did more than help her remember. It helped her recognize where her understanding still had weak joints.

That afternoon the two of them met outside after class, settling onto a bench under a patch of spring shade while students streamed past in FAMU colors, game shirts, and department hoodies.

“You look like you’re arguing with your notes,” Malik said, nodding at her notebook.

“I was,” Nia answered. “They were being vague.”

He laughed. “Did you win?”

“I imposed order.”

“That sounds more like you.”

She glanced at him. “And you?”

He held up his timer. “I’m trying not to let this thing turn me into a worse version of myself.”

Nia smiled. “That might be one of the most honest academic statements anybody has ever made.”

Then her expression softened. “But you’re doing better. I can tell.”

Malik looked out across campus before replying. “I think I finally believe pressure doesn’t have to erase what I know.”

“That,” Nia said, “is a serious shift.”

It was. And they both knew it.

Saturday, April 12, brought homework due, a weekly planning reset, and baseball still at Jackson State. It was the kind of day that could quietly determine the rest of the week if handled well.

Malik went to office hours with targeted questions. He was no longer showing up just to ask what went wrong. He was showing up with evidence. Dr. Price looked over his page and listened as he explained one modeling issue that had bothered him.

When he finished, Dr. Price said, “State assumptions before modeling. Always. If your assumptions are shaky, a polished answer only hides the weakness.”

Malik nodded. “I’ve started hearing that in my head before I even start.”

“Good,” Dr. Price said. “That means you’re developing judgment, not just memory.”

That comment stayed with him long after office hours ended.

Across campus, Nia spent part of the afternoon helping a peer with a difficult concept. The student was frustrated enough to joke about changing majors, but the joke had too much force under it to be harmless. Nia noticed.

“Okay,” she said, pulling out a fresh sheet of paper. “Forget the whole chapter for a second. Tell me the smallest piece you do trust.”

The student frowned, thought, and answered.

“Good,” Nia said. “Now we build from there.”

They worked slowly, layer by layer, until the student’s face changed from defeated to focused.

When they finished, he said, “I thought I was behind everybody.”

Nia shook her head. “A lot of people look calm and are confused. Don’t compare your insides to other people’s outside performance.”

He laughed softly. “You should write that down somewhere.”

“Maybe after finals.”

But helping him also tested her. She could feel how easily one conversation could become three, how naturally she could spend an entire day pouring herself into everybody else’s problems. That was one of the central struggles of her year: not whether she cared, but whether she could care sustainably. So after that session ended, she stopped. She updated her planner, reset her own priorities, and let that count as enough.

Sunday, April 13, brought a new calculus problem set and the familiar Sunday rhythm of preparing before the week could take control. Malik updated his internship and career checklist at a table outside, writing down deadlines, possible summer pathways, and the competencies he was genuinely beginning to embody rather than merely admire from a distance.

Nearby, Nia joined a student organization planning meeting. This time she entered it differently than she might have months earlier. She was enthusiastic, yes, but bounded. Engaged, but intentional.

At one point discussion drifted in six directions at once, and she gently stepped in.

“What has to happen this week?” she asked. “Not eventually. This week.”

The room quieted. Pens moved. Priorities sharpened.

An upperclassman looked at her with open appreciation. “You always bring us back to the center.”

Nia smiled, but the comment affected her more than she showed. All year, she had feared that structure might make her seem less generous, less warm, less expansive. Instead, she was discovering that good structure protected what mattered.

Monday, April 14, arrived with another surge of campus motion. Baseball traveled to the University of North Florida. Softball was at Jacksonville University. Inside the mathematics building and across library floors, however, a different competition was underway: students against distraction, confusion, and late-semester fatigue.

Malik protected another two-hour deep-work block. Nia led another whiteboard study circle. By now their names carried a kind of informal academic credibility among classmates. Not because they were flawless, but because they were consistent.

During the study circle, one of the younger students said, “I feel like everybody else understands faster than I do.”

Before Nia could answer, another student shook her head. “That’s not true. They just start earlier now.”

Nia paused, almost smiling.

That answer told her as much about the culture forming around them as any professor’s praise could have. Students were beginning to teach each other healthier ways of thinking.

“That’s exactly right,” Nia said. “A lot of success looks like being honest early.”

Tuesday, April 15, brought the quiz and concept-check window. The pressure was real, but unlike earlier in the year, it did not feel shapeless. Malik practiced calculus drills with a timer and entered the assessment with steadier breathing than he would have believed possible in the fall. Nia rewrote her notes into one last clean concept map and walked in carrying not just information but perspective.

Afterward they found each other outside the building almost immediately.

“Well?” Nia asked.

Malik let out a breath. “I didn’t rush the first problem.”

She grinned. “Look at you becoming emotionally mature in mathematics.”

He laughed. “I hate how accurate that is.”

“And the rest?”

“Not easy,” he said. “But I stayed with it.”

Nia nodded. “Same. There was one question that tried to scare me into overthinking.”

“Tried?”

“Tried,” she repeated.

There it was again: not perfection, but poise.

Wednesday, April 16, ended the week without spectacle but not without meaning. Malik attended office hours again for targeted questions, and Nia helped another peer with a difficult concept. Dr. Bennett, in a brief conversation that afternoon, reminded Malik to interpret numbers as evidence, not verdicts. That sentence struck deeper than it first appeared. Numbers could inform. They could warn. They could guide. But if handled badly, they could also become emotional judgments people pinned to themselves.

Late that evening Malik and Nia met near the mathematics building as the day gave way to the kind of soft dark that made campus feel reflective rather than crowded. The noise had thinned. The pace had loosened. Windows still glowed in a few rooms where students were finishing work.

For a while they just walked.

“I’ve been thinking,” Malik said at last, “about what that student told me on Thursday.”

“The one who said you explained it like it was fixable?”

He nodded. “Yeah.”

Nia looked ahead. “That was a big sentence.”

“It was.” He adjusted the strap on his bag. “And I think that’s what this week was about. Not just carrying pressure. Carrying it in a way that helps other people believe they can carry theirs too.”

Nia was quiet for a moment.

Then she said, “I think that’s part of what FAMU does when we’re at our best. We don’t just prove we can do hard things. We make hard things feel possible for the next person.”

Malik glanced at her and smiled. “That sounded like a department motto.”

“It should be.”

They kept walking past buildings that had held so much of their year: confusion, breakthroughs, office hours, laughter, low-grade panic, recovered confidence, and the slow construction of identity. Around them FAMU still felt full of motion even in the relative quiet—of mentors investing in students, of undergraduates learning how scholarship and community could reinforce each other, of a mathematics culture that was rigorous without asking anyone to become cold.

By the end of the week, the tension from Week Thirty-Six had found its answer. Yes, Malik and Nia could carry more. Yes, they could help others without immediately losing their own footing. Yes, their growth was becoming visible in the lives around them.

But April was not finished.

As they reached the edge of the walkway and slowed, both of them could feel another kind of pressure gathering just beyond the week they had survived: bigger decisions, end-of-semester consequences, and the approaching moment when habits would have to prove they were strong enough to hold under final strain.

Nia looked toward the math building one last time. “You know what I’m realizing?” she said.

“What?”

“This part of the semester doesn’t just test what you know.” She folded her arms lightly against the evening breeze. “It tests what kind of person your routines have been building.”

Malik let that sit between them.

Then he nodded. “Yeah,” he said. “And I think the next couple of weeks are going to show us the answer.”

That was where the week ended—not in fear, but in sharpened anticipation.

They had learned to carry weight.

Now spring was preparing to reveal just how far that strength could take them.

---

# Week Thirty-Eight: The Shape of What Comes Next

The question waiting at the end of the previous week did not remain unanswered for long.

When Malik and Nia had stood outside the mathematics building and admitted that the final stretch of spring would reveal what kind of people their routines had been building, neither of them had said the next part aloud. They did not need to. Both understood that late April was no longer about potential. It was about proof.

The semester had moved past the stage where good intentions felt impressive. By now, effort had to take form. Assumptions had to be stated. Reasoning had to be shown. Plans had to survive crowded days, noisy campus energy, athletic excitement, deadlines, peer requests, and the quiet emotional fatigue that settled over students when the academic year began leaning heavily toward its close.

And yet, as Friday, April 17 opened over FAMU, the campus did not feel burdened so much as alive.

Baseball was hosting Mississippi Valley State University. Softball was away at Mississippi Valley. Students crossed campus wearing orange and green, carrying backpacks in one hand and weekend plans in the other. The air held that distinct spring mixture of celebration and pressure that FAMU seemed uniquely equipped to carry—joy at the surface, discipline underneath, and community running through both.

For Malik, the first meaningful test of the week arrived in a quieter form.

He sat at a table with his internship and career checklist spread neatly before him, one page covered with dates, another with possible summer opportunities, and a third with handwritten notes about skills he still wanted to sharpen. Earlier in the year, a document like this might have made him anxious. It would have felt like a scoreboard disguised as planning. But something had shifted. The checklist no longer represented a judgment about who he was failing to become. It was evidence of motion.

He reviewed each line with the same seriousness he now brought to mathematics: not as a performance, but as a model that needed clear assumptions.

What did he actually want from the summer?

What opportunities matched the direction he was building toward?

Where was he prepared, and where was he merely hopeful?

He heard Dr. Price’s voice in his head almost immediately: state assumptions before modeling.

So he did.

He wrote at the top of the page:

**Assumptions:** I want work that strengthens quantitative reasoning, communication, and professional confidence. I do not need a perfect opportunity. I need a real one that helps me grow.

Reading that sentence steadied him. It felt less like ambition and more like truth.

Later that afternoon, Nia headed to a student organization planning meeting, and if anyone had watched her closely enough, they would have noticed a quiet difference in the way she entered the room. She still brought energy with her. She still greeted people warmly. She still listened with that alert generosity that made others feel seen. But this version of Nia no longer mistook availability for leadership.

The meeting began the way student meetings often did in April: with too many good ideas, too many upcoming events, and not quite enough structure to protect the strongest ones.

“We could do a recap post from the last event.”

“We should probably start planning finals encouragement bags.”

“What about a collaboration with another org before the semester ends?”

“Also, we still need volunteers for the table next week.”

For a few minutes the conversation scattered in every direction. Then Nia leaned forward.

“Can I try something?” she asked.

Everyone paused.

She uncapped a marker and stood by the board.

“Let’s separate what matters into three questions,” she said. “What must happen this week? What would help if we have the capacity? And what should wait until we can actually do it well?”

The room quieted in the particular way it does when structure arrives right on time.

An upperclassman smiled. “Please keep talking.”

Nia laughed softly and began making columns.

By the end of the meeting, the chaos had become a plan. Tasks were assigned. Deadlines were realistic. No one seemed less enthusiastic—just less scattered. As they packed up, another student said, “You always make things feel possible without making them feel small.”

That stayed with Nia long after she left.

It was one of the clearest signs yet that the tension from the previous week had found its resolution. She had worried all year that if she became more selective, more bounded, more intentional, she might somehow become less herself. Instead, she was learning that disciplined care could be even more powerful than endless availability.

Saturday, April 18 carried more sports energy across campus as baseball faced Mississippi Valley State again and softball remained on the road. The weekend atmosphere might have invited distraction, but Malik and Nia had both learned how to enjoy campus life without surrendering their academic center.

Malik claimed a two-hour deep-work block in the library, arriving with his materials organized before most students had fully settled into the day. The library had become one of his clearest symbols of transformation. In the fall, he had sometimes entered spaces like this carrying tension so tightly that every problem felt like a threat. Now he entered with a system.

He chose a table near a window, set a timer, and began working.

Midway through the session, he got stuck—not because the problem was impossible, but because it invited the old temptation to push forward with incomplete understanding. He caught himself almost immediately.

He stopped.

He rewrote the setup.

He identified what was known.

He stated the assumptions.

Only then did he proceed.

The solution unfolded cleanly after that.

Malik leaned back, not triumphant, but quietly satisfied. The real victory was not that he had gotten the answer. The real victory was that he had noticed the danger early enough to change course. That was maturity. That was transfer. That was the kind of growth professors hoped for when they repeated the same principles often enough for students to carry them into independent work.

Across campus, Nia led a whiteboard study circle that turned into one of the most revealing gatherings of the spring.

Students arrived in waves, some carrying notebooks, some carrying snacks, some carrying expressions that announced they were more worried than they wanted to admit. Nia greeted each of them with easy warmth, then turned to the board.

“Today,” she said, “we’re not just solving problems. We’re practicing how to stay clear when the problem stops looking familiar.”

A few students nodded immediately.

One of them groaned. “So basically the exact thing I’ve been struggling with.”

“Perfect,” Nia said. “Then you’re in the right room.”

The group laughed, and the tension broke.

She started with a simple problem and then deliberately changed the framing.

“What changed?” she asked.

“The wording.”

“The layout.”

“It looks less obvious.”

“Good,” Nia said. “Now tell me what did **not** change.”

That question slowed everyone down in the best way.

Soon the board filled with arrows, sketches, conceptual labels, and small phrases that linked procedures back to meaning. Nia did not rush to the answer. She kept redirecting the room toward structure.

“What is the concept underneath this version?”

“What relationship still holds?”

“How would you explain this to someone who froze for a minute?”

At one point a first-year student admitted, “When I can’t see it right away, I start feeling like everybody else is ahead of me.”

Before Nia spoke, another student answered from across the room.

“Everybody else is usually just hiding it better.”

That drew laughter, but it also drew recognition.
